From mikeraz at patch.com  Thu Dec  1 12:03:07 2005
From: mikeraz at patch.com (Michael Rasmussen)
Date: Thu, 1 Dec 2005 12:03:07 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Configuring MySQL to use PHP
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0512011155140.3073@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0512011155140.3073@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <20051201200307.GB16772@patch.com>

Rich Shepard wrote:
>   Will someone here tell me how to check if MySQL-5.0 is set up to use PHP?
> And, if not, how I properly configure it.

It's the other way around, PHP will use MySQL, or Postgres, or whatever.

You use a RedHat varient?  `rpm -qa | grep php` will show you if
the package php-mysql package is installed, or the php4-mysql one. 

Debian varient?  `dpkg --list | grep php`

After that check /etc/php.ini for MySQL config options.

-- 
    Michael Rasmussen, Portland Oregon

        http://www.patch.com/words/

		-- Mark Twain

Thanks for passing that on, Michael. Jeff also said that he would  
edit the video and put up that as well. He probably won't get back to  
it until next week sometime.

I spent the day at the Real Time Conference. I went to the Linux  
sessions. Monte Vista and TimeSys weren't there, so they couldn't  
tell us about their patches to the kernel for hard realtime, so the  
Blue Cat Linux folk (LynuxWorks SOUNDS like a Linux supporter,  
doesn't it?) were busy telling us that Linux is unsuitable for hard  
realtime. So what happened to the RT patch? Well, a lot of it got  
folded into the 2.6 kernel, they acknowledged, but Linux is only  
suitable for soft RT. I hung around for the talk on changes to the  
2.6 kernel that were specific to realtime, but the marketing guy get  
babbling and there was some poor benighted soul from IBM who kept  
trying to correct him (I know he meant well, but he was trying to get  
a *marketing* guy to get his facts straight and, well, he was a  
*marketing* guy so...). And whenever the FAE tried to get onto a  
track that might be even slightly interesting, the marketing guy  
brought it back on message. BUY OUR STUFF! Geez. What could have been  
an interesting talk...

Enough bitching and moaning.

-Ron

On Thu, 1 Dec 2005, Michael Rasmussen wrote:

> What is the distinction between hard and soft RT?  Does, for instance, soft
> RT have enough Real in the Time to work for telescope control?

   I assumed when I read this that "hard RT" is what they do and "soft RT" is
what everyone else does. That way they can claim a unique position in the
marketplace.

   In my opinion, unless there's an earthquake going on, telescope control is
at a much slower Real Time than shutting down a run-away nuclear reactor
core.

   As some sage wrote here not too long ago, "real time" is fast enough for
whatever data stream you need to capture or control. It's a subjective term
that's dependent on the context.

Rich

On Dec 1, 2005, at 5:20 PM, Michael Rasmussen wrote:
> Ron Braithwaite wrote:
>> were busy telling us that Linux is unsuitable for hard
>> realtime. So what happened to the RT patch? Well, a lot of it got
>> folded into the 2.6 kernel, they acknowledged, but Linux is only
>> suitable for soft RT.
>
> What is the distiction between hard and soft RT?  Does, for instance,
> soft RT have enough Real in the Time to work for telescope control?

The difference is pretty much a matter of deterministic behavior and  
is also relative. If an interrupt (or some other event that I have  
defined) comes in, can I be absolutely certain that the system will  
respond within an acceptable time.

More years ago than I care to remember, I had a project that weighed  
rail cars and guessed what kind of cars they were as they slowly (<5  
mph) moved across a section of track that had strain gage. For that  
purpose, I needed to be absolutely certain that I logged the data  
within 1 second (or so - it's been 25 years or so). Technically, this  
was hard realtime since I would lose data if the interrupt latency &  
context switch took more than a second. Realistically, a soft RT  
system would work here, because just about anything other than an  
abacus can be guaranteed to respond within a second.

When looking at a realtime system, there is the best case, average,  
and worst case times for response times. The worst case is probably  
pathological - pretty rare. So if what happens in that worse case? If  
people die or airplanes fall out of the sky, that's a case for a hard  
realtime system. On the other hand, if the average response time is  
well within tolerance and the result of a worst case response time is  
just annoy (say, you change the channel on the remote and it doesn't  
happen), soft realtime is acceptable.

And then there's the whole issue of the priority inversion problem,  
but friends have just come over for dinner and I'm being told to get  
of the &*()@#$ computer and be social.

  Hope that answers your question. I can drone on ad nauseam, of course.

-Ron

Ron Braithwaite
2015 NE 37th Ave
Portland, OR 97212 USA
503-267-3250
ron at braithwaites.net




From aaron at bavariati.org  Thu Dec  1 18:56:27 2005
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Thu, 1 Dec 2005 18:56:27 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Jeff Waugh podcast - on to Real Time
In-Reply-To: <20051202012056.GA24994@patch.com>
References: <20051201181510.70705.qmail@web52703.mail.yahoo.com>
	<20051201195607.GA16772@patch.com>
	<0F8AF6FE-3C67-44A5-AE28-EA886F0C4EA3@braithwaites.net>
	<20051202012056.GA24994@patch.com>
Message-ID: <20051202025627.GX18419@bavariati.org>

On Thu, Dec 01, 2005 at 05:20:56PM -0800, Michael Rasmussen wrote:
> Ron Braithwaite wrote:
> > were busy telling us that Linux is unsuitable for hard  
> > realtime. So what happened to the RT patch? Well, a lot of it got  
> > folded into the 2.6 kernel, they acknowledged, but Linux is only  
> > suitable for soft RT. 

I'd agree.  IMO Linux is too complex and powerful to be hard RT.
Leave that to the PLCs and hand-programmed microcontrollers.

> What is the distiction between hard and soft RT?  Does, for instance,
> soft RT have enough Real in the Time to work for telescope control?

As far as I know: 
Low-latency means most interrupts will mostly be serviced within about
X microseconds.  This is good for user experience.

Soft RT guarantees that interrupts will always be serviced within X
microseconds, which is important for many SW and HW applications.

Hard RT guarantees that a particular interrupt will always be serviced
in no more than X microseconds and no less than Y microseconds.  This
deterministic behavior is critical in systems where the controller's
latency is part of the system (or safety) design.

On Dec 1, 2005, at 9:59 PM, Ted Kubaska wrote:
> I was talking with Jeff Waugh and Ron Braithwiate about Ubuntu in  
> the Lucky
> Lab. Ron mentioned that yes Ubuntu runs under VPC but you have to  
> make a
> command line adjustment during install. I didn't write it down,  
> thought I'd
> remember it; it was pretty straightforward. Well, I forgot. Can you  
> remind
> me? Thanks.

Dammit, I'm not at my Linux box and my wife is asleep across the room  
from it. I apologize in advance for any lapsed synapses in my memory  
after several tasty beers with friends.

So, with that caveat in mind, let me go from memory. It seems that  
Virtual PC only supports 16 bit color (the shame) and Ubuntu comes up  
in 24 bit mode - they've got this really smart installer that made  
one too many assumptions (it really is good, though) - and you've got  
to tell X to start in 16 bit mode or things go all whacko on the screen.

So fire up your box and don't start X. From your nice non-GUI trip  
back in memory lane, sudo vi /etc/X11/xorg.conf (did I get that  
right? It's the X config file, dammit), and change the color depth  
(sorry, I can't remember the arg, but it will be obvious) from 24 to  
16. Start X (or reboot or whatever) and everything should be fine. It  
is usable on my 800 MHz G3 iBook and almost (but not quite) snappy on  
the 2 GHz G5.

Hot rumor (actually just my conjecture about what's right over the  
horizon): Apple's new machines with the Intel processors will have  
that cool new hardware virtualization which will be fully supported  
under OS X 10.4.5. We will be able to run Linux and WinXP alongside  
OS X without any penalties. Without buying any other product. After  
all, Apple is a hardware company and they've got the most exquisitely  
beautiful user interface - virtualization built in will sell a BUNCH  
of hardware.

This is going to be GREAT for Linux. Right now, Gnome & KDE are  
rapidly evolving into really nice interfaces, but most people don't  
see them (or OS X, for that matter) because they only use one OS. And  
that's because there are still a lot of programs out there that only  
run on one of those platforms, so a machine that supports all three  
(or more) operating systems will give us the opportunity to try  
something new without abandoning the old.

Most of the rapid evolution in Gnome, KDE, and the F/OSS apps comes  
from us (as the users and as the actors and as the greek chorus). A  
lot of our designs come from remembering other work we've seen or  
other ideas we've encountered and then improving on it. Having an  
Intel PowerBook where I can use cool new tools to build cross- 
platform apps and then *rapidly* look at them under Gnome, KDE, OS X,  
and Win XP, means that my user interface designs (as crude as they  
may be) will probably evolve quickly into something less  
objectionable. I think it entirely possible that this development  
will lead to Gnome and KDE out-evolving even OS X and that the  
"masses" will realize that the whole M$ architecture sucks as badly  
as their business model.

But in the long run, the WinXP, OS X, and Linux arguments don't make  
any difference. Because people won't choose Linux because of our  
arguments. It will be because of the quality of our work and how  
intuitive we make our user interface(s) will push people in our  
direction over the long run. Eventually, the choice of operating  
system is irrelevant and people will end up choosing the best  
operating system based on what they think makes it best.

In that case, Linux and OS X will try to out-evolve each other and  
WinXP will fade just as GEM once did (it'll take longer to fade away,  
but it will happen if there are real alternatives). With a 6 month  
release cycle time for both Gnome and Ubuntu and with Apple on a 1  
year cycle, we are going to end up with some really cool stuff in the  
very near future.

I'm excited - we are about to see whether the Open Source development  
model really is more productive and rock-solid than even the most  
talented and motivated proprietary model.

And I haven't even started raving about Harmony & Mono, yet.

> Ron, you should check this site out...

Ron Braithwaite wrote:

> I spent the day at the Real Time Conference. I went to the Linux

> doesn't it?) were busy telling us that Linux is unsuitable for hard  
> realtime. So what happened to the RT patch? Well, a lot of it got  
> folded into the 2.6 kernel, they acknowledged, but Linux is only  
> suitable for soft RT. I hung around for the talk on changes to the  
> 2.6 kernel that were specific to realtime, but the marketing guy get  
> babbling and there was some poor benighted soul from IBM who kept  
> trying to correct him (I know he meant well, but he was trying to get

I haven't visited their site(s) recently, but a couple of years ago 
there was a lot of interesting "hard real-time" kernel work going on in 
Dresden. There's a microkernel (L4) and a Linux layer on top of the 
microkernel. Last time I looked they had 2.6, 2.4 and 2.2 kernels on top 
of the DROPS (Dresden Real-Time Operating System). Anyhow, the URL is

http://os.inf.tu-dresden.de/drops/

Last posting there is from August 2004 -- maybe this died :(.

and add apache and databases or install in server mode and add desktop
aps (and maybe disable a few services)?

I'm also trying to decide if there's any reason to install both Fedora
and Ubuntu (the disk is 18Gig).

Any advice or pointers would also be appreciated.

From creswick at gmail.com  Fri Dec  2 09:55:15 2005
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Fri, 2 Dec 2005 09:55:15 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] nerd mode for installing Ubuntu 5.10?
In-Reply-To: <20051202173952.6833.qmail@web52704.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20051202173952.6833.qmail@web52704.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <c834ec80512020955j6e70142aw7c0b1bc90aba0f0c@mail.gmail.com>

On 12/2/05, Jon LeVitre <jonlevitre at yahoo.com> wrote:
> Can anybody tell me whether it'll be easier to install in desktop mode
> and add apache and databases or install in server mode and add desktop
> aps (and maybe disable a few services)?

I have a similar setup, but I don't recall how I did the install. 
I'll advocate doing a desktop-centric install initially, then adding
the packages you want over telling the installer you want a
server-centric install, and removing things.  Generally you're safer
adding services you want than removing those you don't need, in the
later case there are likely to be services running you aren't aware of
and don't need.

Either way, it would probably be wise to severly restrict the
connections allowed to your laptop.  Denying anything other than ssh
from hosts other than localhost may be a good place to start. 
/etc/host.deny or some firewall app should do nicely.

> I'm also trying to decide if there's any reason to install both Fedora
> and Ubuntu (the disk is 18Gig).

IMHO, there is no reason :) particularly with a disk that size.

--Rogan

> Any advice or pointers would also be appreciated.

From mikeraz at patch.com  Fri Dec  2 10:07:33 2005
From: mikeraz at patch.com (Michael Rasmussen)
Date: Fri, 2 Dec 2005 10:07:33 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] SugarCRM: The Saga Continues
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0512020804580.9119@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0512020701300.9119@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<20051202154339.GC28171@patch.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.63.0512020804580.9119@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <25907.170.135.241.46.1133546853.squirrel@mail.patch.com>


Rich Shepard wrote:
>    I sent this directly to you because attachments are stripped by the mail
> list server. However, the rest of the message may make it onto the list.
>
>    "Not available;" see attached screenshot.

snif, snif, do I smell a permissions issue?

At some point you had to give Sugar an ID and password for accessing the MySQL
system.

If it didn't ask for the root access information, you would have needed to create
the account through MySQL.

Typically the instructions to do so follow the form:

(commands with prompts and feedback left out)

  mysqladmin -u root -p create DATABASENAME
  mysql -u root -p
  grant ALL on DATABASENAME.* to SugarID identified by 'secretive';

If all of that is totally unfamiliar in this context I suspect that the database
is not available because Sugar does not have any access rights to the database and
MySQL is refusing access.

   Michael Rasmussen, Portland, Ore, USA
  Be Appropriate && Follow Your Curiosity
        http://www.patch.com/words/




From mikeraz at patch.com  Fri Dec  2 10:16:43 2005
From: mikeraz at patch.com (Michael Rasmussen)
Date: Fri, 2 Dec 2005 10:16:43 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] nerd mode for installing Ubuntu 5.10?
In-Reply-To: <20051202173952.6833.qmail@web52704.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20051202173952.6833.qmail@web52704.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <29916.170.135.241.46.1133547403.squirrel@mail.patch.com>


Jon LeVitre wrote:
> Can anybody tell me whether it'll be easier to install in desktop mode
> and add apache and databases or install in server mode and add desktop
> aps (and maybe disable a few services)?

Server mode install doesn't do X.  So I'd suggest desktop mode and then add what
you want for services.

> I'm also trying to decide if there's any reason to install both Fedora
> and Ubuntu (the disk is 18Gig).

Being able to work in both environments will help you understand the underlying
workings without getting dependent on implementation details.  The way the two
handle, for instance, network setup and initialization is quite different.

If you do go that route, plan out your disk partitioning carefully. You'll 
undoubtedly what a separate and shared /home partition.

If you go app install crazy the 18G may become a restrictive amount of space. My
10G drive notebook is requiring care to avoid running out of disk space.

--
   Michael Rasmussen, Portland, Ore, USA
  Be Appropriate && Follow Your Curiosity
        http://www.patch.com/words/

A bunch of us are doing this at work.  We all started with the desktop
setup (with 5.04, actually, followed by an 'apt-get dist-upgrade',
since that was the version we had a CD for) then added servers as
needed.  So far it's been working fine.

I'm a long-time Debian user.  The only place I've seen Ubuntu fall
short of Debian is in the packages for cross-compiling PocketPC apps
-- not exactly a frequently traveled corner of the distributions. :-)

From iconoklastic at yahoo.com  Fri Dec  2 12:18:13 2005
From: iconoklastic at yahoo.com (Robert Kopp)
Date: Fri, 2 Dec 2005 12:18:13 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Dreamweaver
Message-ID: <20051202201813.5839.qmail@web50608.mail.yahoo.com>

Although I do not use Dreamweaver myself, because
hand-coding suffices for me, I've heard that the
absence of a Linux equivalent is the main reason why
many Linux users still require a dual-boot system. I
suppose Dreamweaver launches on vmware, but then you
still need a Windows licence (at least in theory) and
a somewhat spendy program. (Of course, Dreamweaver
itself, if legally obtained, isn't cheap.) Some
designers find that Bluefish meets their needs, but
progress on the Dreamweaver front is probably needed
to shrink Windows' installed base.

Robert "Tim" Kopp
http://analytic.tripod.com/

mysql> SET PASSWORD FOR ''@'localhost' = PASSWORD('<password>');

Step 6: Perform Setup   SugarCRM
Creating the config file...done

Creating the database sugarcrm on localhost...done
Creating the db username and password...Creating the db username and password
for localhost...done

Unable to select database: Access denied for user ''@'localhost' to database
'sugarcrm'

setting it for a server, not a user. Yet, the sugarcrm message tells me that

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Sat Dec  3 10:18:10 2005
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason R. Martin)
Date: Sat, 3 Dec 2005 10:18:10 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Securing Initial Accounts in MySQL
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0512021246540.9119@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0512021246540.9119@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <c29537840512031018o775ed888xceb5f88ddfe57dcc@mail.gmail.com>

On 12/2/05, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>    Page 125 of the mysql-4.1 manual (section 2.9.3: Securing Initial Accounts)
> tells me to start the database interface, then use
>
> mysql> SET PASSWORD FOR ''@'localhost' = PASSWORD('<password>');
> mysql> SET PASSWORD FOR ''@'<hostname>' = PASSWORD('<password>');
>
> which is what I did.
>
>    Then, running the SugarCRM setup wizard I get to the last page where all
> the options and configuation choices have been entered and the application is
> supposed to create the database, my user account, and start running. Well, it
> can't even get up on its hands and knees and crawl, never mind run.
>
>    What I see in the dialog box is this:
>
> Step 6: Perform Setup   SugarCRM
> Creating the config file...done
>
> Installation is proceeding... the output will appear below once it is
> complete. Please wait.
> Creating the database sugarcrm on localhost...done
> Creating the db username and password...Creating the db username and password
> for localhost...done
>
> Unable to select database: Access denied for user ''@'localhost' to database
> 'sugarcrm'
>
>    Now, simple-minded as I am, when I set the mysql password, I thought I was
> setting it for a server, not a user. Yet, the sugarcrm message tells me that
> there's a user called ''@'localhost'.

You can define different passwords (and privileges) for the same
username based on the host it logs in from, but you still need to have
a username.  '%' is the host wildcard.  What you probably want
(thought I don't know anything about sugarCRM) is something like user
sugarcrm and host localhost, i.e. 'sugarcrm'@'localhost', though if it
doesn't matter what system the web application is running on you could
simplify things with 'sugarcrm'@'%'.

>    Is this a mysql error on my part? In the sugarCRM setup, I defined myself
> as the authorized user, and the username 'mysql' as the db admin.

You want to create a user for the *application* and grant it the
permissions the application needs.  You can then define yourself as an
admin.  Make sure you change the password for 'root' as well :-)

>    With hope that this is the last hurdle to overcome, I ask for another clue.

Hopefully that's a clue.  But MySQL is getting hazy in my gray matter...

Jason

Ken,

   I certainly don't have the breadth of experience that you do. I suspect
that the SugarCRM company is not "small" as is Dieter Simander of SQL-Ledger
or Patrick Volkerding of Slackware. The company had a large booth at the
OSCON exhibition with several sugar cubes in branded corporate uniform. I
assume they have some VC money behind them.

> I work with great egos all the time.  Most of them are good people with
> good intentions, but often their opinions get in my way. I am sure glad I
> don't have a great ego or any opinions. 87)

   Well, a great ego is not difficult to develop; there are a bunch of
self-help books out that you can read for guidance. Or, go to a city council,
county commission, or state legislative session and observe the clash of egos
on a grand scale. If you're lazy, just look for those who put academic
degrees or the initials of other certifications after their names and you'll
find excellent examples of great egos.

   I also suspect that you're overlooking some opionions stuck in the corners
of your pockets or left over from previous jobs. The really dangerous aspect
of having opinions is expressing them in the wrong forum or in front of
people who take umbrage that you actually have opinions -- and they're
different from theirs.

   Anyway, SugarCRM got past that sticking point (thanks to Kurt), and has now
been "Populating the database tables with demo data (this may take a little
while)..." for over 10 minutes. Sigh. It's actually fighting me; perhaps it
just doesn't want to run here.

Thanks,

Rich

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Sat Dec  3 13:05:48 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Sat, 3 Dec 2005 13:05:48 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Closing the Thread: SugarCRM
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0512031254550.4677@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   I removed all traces of SugarCRM-3.5.1a and MySQL-4.1.14 from my system ...
er, my computer system, that is. Then I reinstalled the mysql package and ran
'mysql_install_db --user=mysql' and the root password change, followed by
/etc/rc.d/rc.mysqld start. That spawned a whole slew of mysqld processes ...
and all was Good.

   Next, I downloaded and installed the sugarcrm zip file. Ran through the
configuration again, This time I left off loading the demo tables and the
setup went through like a load of Exlax. And all was Good, again. I even read
the note that told me I needed to log in as the admin the first time. So I
did that.

   As admin, I have nothing there and I really don't want to use the
application as admin. So, I logged out and tried logging in as a mere mortal
user. Not Good. Despite the setup being told my username and password, it
won't take it. However, it will take the admin username and that password.

   This is certainly a Windows-centric application. For example, if I want to
add me as an employee, one of the fields is "IM Type". I figured out that
stands for "Instant Messaging"; the choices are MSN, Yahoo!, and AOL. Feh!

   Anyway, thank you all for your patient help and good advice. I'll play with
this and see if it's really goining to be useful despite the feature glut for
a one-person shop.

Rich

Rich Shepard wrote:
> On Sat, 3 Dec 2005, Michael Rasmussen wrote:
> >Just a sanity check, you are aware that the permissions and userID and
> >password system for MySQL has nothing to do with the userID and password
> >you use to access the computer. ??
> 
>   Yes. I'm writing about the ability to access the SugarCRM application as a
> user (defined in /var/www/htdocs/sugarCRM-3.5.1a/config.php) rather than as

Rich, I was asking in light of this statement:

  So, I logged out and tried logging in as a mere mortal user. Not
  Good. Despite the setup being told my username and password, it won't
  take it. However, it will take the admin username and that password.

Is YOUR[1] username and password configured in the MySQL security system?  
MySQL does not care what is stored in /etc/passwd and /etc/shadow.
If you can log in as admin and not as the "mere mortal" it indicates
that "mere mortal" isn not defined in the MySQL system.

[1] Where YOUR equals "a mere mortal user".

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Sat Dec  3 15:26:59 2005
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Sat, 03 Dec 2005 15:26:59 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Closing the Thread: SugarCRM
In-Reply-To: <20051203230304.GA3140@patch.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0512031254550.4677@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<20051203211840.GA755@patch.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.63.0512031325480.4677@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<20051203230304.GA3140@patch.com>
Message-ID: <1133652419.24307.10.camel@denk.nakedape.cc>

On Sat, 2005-12-03 at 15:03 -0800, Michael Rasmussen wrote:

> Rich, I was asking in light of this statement:
> 
>   So, I logged out and tried logging in as a mere mortal user. Not
>   Good. Despite the setup being told my username and password, it won't
>   take it. However, it will take the admin username and that password.
> 
> Is YOUR[1] username and password configured in the MySQL security system?  
> MySQL does not care what is stored in /etc/passwd and /etc/shadow.
> If you can log in as admin and not as the "mere mortal" it indicates
> that "mere mortal" isn not defined in the MySQL system.
> 
> [1] Where YOUR equals "a mere mortal user".

Let's be careful here--there are three layers of authentication and it
is important not to get them confused:
  1. System authentication - "Regular" user accounts (in /etc/passwd)
  2. Database authentication - Who can connect to the database directly 
     (rows in mysql.Users)
  3. Application authentication - Accounts within the application
itself, stored in tables in the app's database

Rich: I assume you've logged into the application using whatever default
admin account it setup and created a user account for yourself.  If not,
this is the piece you're missing.  (You have to do the same thing with
SQL-Ledger, so it should be familiar, although it's probably been a
while and you might've forgotten.)


Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley                                 wcooley at nakedape.cc

From mikeraz at patch.com  Sun Dec  4 06:45:56 2005
From: mikeraz at patch.com (Michael Rasmussen)
Date: Sun, 4 Dec 2005 06:45:56 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] apt-get upgrade failure ... resolved
In-Reply-To: <20051204125708.GA20723@patch.com>
References: <20051204125708.GA20723@patch.com>
Message-ID: <20051204144556.GA26714@patch.com>

Michael Rasmussen wrote:
> I'm in a locked up situation with a dist-upgrade to current Ubuntu
> realease, 
> How do I correct this situation?  Attempting to remove gconf2-common
> also errors out.

Found help elsewhere:
Seth Hasani wrote:
> > How do I correct this situation?  Attempting to remove gconf2-common
> > also errors out.
>
> Oh God I hate when that happens! Sometimes on certain dist-upgrades
> apt
> needs a kick. What I did to resolve it was running:
>     dpkg -i --force-overwrite /var/cache/apt/archives/$badpackage.deb
> where $badpackage.deb was whatever package was giving problems. After
> that, dist-upgrade usually goes on without a hitch. Use --force-overwrite
> with caution though.

Seth had it correct.  I had to do a --force-overwrite with two packages
followed by "apt-get -f install" (yes, with no packages listed) to flush
out some other lingering dependencies.

The update is now complete.

Note about his word of caution:  --force-overwrite could install
incompatible to the point of deadly versions of files.  I went ahead
with it because the files in question were Gnome related and I knew this
system would remain in console mode until after I completed a full and
successful upgrade.  I could work knowing that anything broken in Gnome
wouldn't bring down the system.

-- 
    Michael Rasmussen, Portland Oregon

I suspect location is the problem. I sent Comcast tech support an e-mail 
from their web site. The response was fairly quick, but didn't resolve 
anything. The tech did try to be helpful, though:

Comcast Portland wrote:

> I understand you are having issues with your Internet connection.  After
> reviewing your account information, and your location, I do not see any
> outages in your area affecting connectivity. Just to make sure, I ran a
> couple of tests from here to your modem, and did not get any slow or
> missing responses. Slow or missing responses would need to be present to
> indicate a connectivity issue. 
> 
> If you are using any additional networking hardware to connect your
> computer to the modem, remove that equipment and connect the computer
> directly to the modem and see if the connectivity issue still occurs.
> If you no longer experience connectivity issue, the problem is with the
> networking equipment, and you will need to contact the hardware manufacturer. 

The connection has been stable all afternoon (so far), so I thanked him 
for his efforts and said I'd give his recommendation a try if I 
experienced further problems. Of course, that would make it difficult 
for my wife to connect, and I'd be without such firewall as my Netgear 
RT314 provides, so I wouldn't run that test for very long. Besides, now 
that I think about it, there doesn't seem to be any problem with the 
RT314 -- that is, I don't think it can cause the cable modem to go 
through a connect sequence.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Dec  5 17:20:02 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Mon, 5 Dec 2005 17:20:02 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Comcast troubles
In-Reply-To: <43949B6D.3010306@comcast.net>
References: <43949B6D.3010306@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20051206012002.GA31953@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Mon, Dec 05, 2005 at 11:56:29AM -0800, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> Has anyone else been having trouble with Comcast this morning? Several 
> times, I've looked over at the cable modem and found it going through 
> the sequence it uses to establish a connection. This seems to happen 
> shortly after I've successfully received some e-mail, and am trying to 
> send a reply.
> 
> The solution has been to power down the cable modem for 10 seconds or so 
> and then power it back up.

I've been downloading through Comcast all day.  So somewhere between
you and the Phat Pipe (possibly even your computer or your power feed)
something is wonky.  From my firewall:

[root]# ifconfig eth0
eth0      Link encap:Ethernet  HWaddr xx:xx:xx:xx:xx:xx
          inet addr:xx.xx.xxx.xx  Bcast:255.255.255.255  Mask:255.255.248.0
          UP BROADCAST RUNNING MULTICAST  MTU:1500  Metric:1
          RX packets:197946757 errors:7721 dropped:0 overruns:7722 frame:7721
          TX packets:28652603 errors:0 dropped:0 overruns:0 carrier:1
          collisions:0 txqueuelen:1000
          RX bytes:2671832718 (2548.0 Mb)  TX bytes:991186072 (945.2 Mb)
          Interrupt:3 Base address:0x300

[root]# uptime
17:04:29  up 103 days, 18:08,  1 user,  load average: 0.10, 0.05, 0.01

Assuming that you have a linux box talking to the cable modem, you might
look at ethereal or tcpdump output and see if somebody else is dragging
down the line, or attacking your box.  You might also put a cheap little
4 port ethernet hub between your computer and the cable modem, so you
can watch the blinky lights, or monitor the traffic with another computer.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From chaz at bctonline.com  Mon Dec  5 20:13:10 2005
From: chaz at bctonline.com (Charles Sliger)

In-Reply-To: <1133330596.438d40a4ecdfc@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <B0015345059@bct-mail.bctonline.com>

I'm not a postfix hack, but shouldn't postfix be able to perform a reverse
DNS lookup in conjunction with a white-list to determine if the site is
legit?
Please share the answer when you determine what can be done.  I'm looking at
using postfix myself.

Charles L. Sliger,    Information Systems Engineer,    chaz at bctonline.com
"no matter where you go, there you are..."

-----Original Message-----
From: plug-bounces at lists.pdxlinux.org
[mailto:plug-bounces at lists.pdxlinux.org] On Behalf Of
plug_0 at robinson-west.com
Sent: Tuesday, November 29, 2005 10:03 PM
To: plug at lists.pdxlinux.org
Subject: [PLUG] Scope for a Postfix server...

The default for postfix 2.x seems to be, "talk with any smtp server that 
can see me."  A postfix smtp site can accept data from smtp servers 
that can see it even if it can't reach them.  Oops, this can lead to
a clogged defer queue incredibly fast.

This problem could be solved by having the postfix site, site A,
use a map of the world that is limited so it can refuse to 
communicate with servers it can't reach. 
 
                        public  |  private
     /----------\               |
     | INTERNET |            /-----\          /---\
     |          | ---------- |  C  |----------| A |
     | /----\   |            \-----/          \---/
     | | B  |   |               |
     | \----/   |               |
     \----------/               |

Site A is hidden on a private network hosted by the gateway, Site C.

There is one host on the Internet that is supposed to be able to
reach site A over it.  Unfortunately, this host uses a dynamic ip.

I don't want ratholes jamming garbage into Site A.  Site A trys to 
bounce the garbage back and then defers it when it can't connect
to the ratholes.  I only want to service host B off of the net.  
Site A should not defer anything to/from the Internet that is
not to/from Site B.

Since Site A firewalls outbound smtp requests to it's local subnet 
and site B, one thought is to have a parallel change occur at site 
C.  Have site C do destination nat for site B only.  Can't do it, 
easytcp on site C and easytcp on site A are incompatible.  For
now, site C does destination nat for site B's subnet, 65k hosts.

Even if the firewalls at Site C and Site A change in parallel,
what's to stop jamming of the defer queue at site A if a local 
user attempts to email a site that site A can resolve, but not 
connect to?  

Can postfix know that a site it's trying to reach is blocked 
for a reason and not just down?  Do I solve the worldview problem
at site A by doing parallel packet filter changes at site A and
site C?  Do I need to configure site A in a special way?

Masquerading and destination nat can get you into another ip 
network, but information is lost at the destination concerning 
the true source of your packets.

From mikeraz at patch.com  Mon Dec  5 21:33:54 2005

	<B0015345059@bct-mail.bctonline.com>

Charles Sliger wrote:
> I'm not a postfix hack, but shouldn't postfix be able to perform a reverse
> DNS lookup in conjunction with a white-list to determine if the site is
> legit?

You mean it must fulfill either requirement or one of the two
requirements?

Be forewarned that enough mail servers are not resolvable through
reverse lookup to make it a frustrating requirement for OKing mail.
One reference to the issues is
http://www.saas.nsw.edu.au/solutions/dns.html

From chaz at bctonline.com  Tue Dec  6 09:03:19 2005

what I meant was that postfix would get the system/domain name via a reverse
DNS lookup and then look up that name in a white-list to see if it should be
allowed through.  That way you are dealing with a persistent name instead of
a changing address.  This would seem to be a viable solution since there are
very few servers you wish to accept mail from out on the internet.

> reverse lookup to make it a frustrating requirement for OKing mail.

From publiustemp-plug at yahoo.com  Tue Dec  6 12:48:22 2005
From: publiustemp-plug at yahoo.com (Ovid)
Date: Tue, 6 Dec 2005 12:48:22 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Perl Foundation News
Message-ID: <20051206204822.82984.qmail@web60819.mail.yahoo.com>

Hi all,

For those interested in Perl and the Perl community, you may be
interested to know that the Perl Foundation now has a blog at
http://news.perlfoundation.org/.

Rather than being the driving force behind Perl, we want the community
to be the driving force.  As a result, we post what we're doing and we
solicit feedback, commentary, questions, etc.  Please let us know what
we're doing right and wrong and what you'd like us to do in the future.

Cheers,
Ovid

-- 
If this message is a response to a question on a mailing list, please send
follow up questions to the list.

From mikeraz at patch.com  Tue Dec  6 13:31:31 2005
From: mikeraz at patch.com (Michael Rasmussen)
Date: Tue, 6 Dec 2005 13:31:31 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Software You Need to Have
Message-ID: <25518.170.135.241.46.1133904691.squirrel@mail.patch.com>


Hone those problem solving skills:

 http://trouble-maker.sourceforge.net/

There are a lot of tools out there to make the system administrator's life easier.
However, no tool is a replacement for properly understanding the system and
experience in troubleshooting unknown situations. This is where trouble-maker
comes in. Unlike other projects, we do not attempt to solve problems -- we cause
them.

When installed and run, this project will randomly select a problem from its set
of issues and make it happen on your system. This can give you experience dealing
with:

    * Dealing with partially accurate user reporting of problems
    * Troubleshooting boot problems
    * Troubleshooting service configuration problems
    * Troubleshooting (simulated) hardware problems

-- 
   Michael Rasmussen, Portland, Ore, USA
  Be Appropriate && Follow Your Curiosity
        http://www.patch.com/words/

From plug at merlot.com  Wed Dec  7 07:56:16 2005
From: plug at merlot.com (Kurt Sussman)
Date: Wed, 7 Dec 2005 07:56:16 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Arrrrrrggg! Problems with php5 and freetype
In-Reply-To: <2539.67.42.83.21.1133934574.squirrel@botteronet.net>
References: <2539.67.42.83.21.1133934574.squirrel@botteronet.net>
Message-ID: <20051207155616.GM2586@merlot.com>

jjb at botteronet.net (jjb at botteronet.net) typed this ...
> Any idea where I should look for a problem, what problems might
> possibly
> effect my system in this way? I'm about to give up on it...

Find the gd.so file (assuming you built it as a plugin, use the php
apache plugin file otherwise, probably libphp5.so) and run ldd on it:

  $ ldd /usr/lib/php4/20050606/gd.so

You'll see a line that starts 'libfreetype.so.6 =>'. What it points at
will tell you whether it's finding the freetype library.

If not, that's an important clue. If it is finding the library or if it
doesn't have a reference to it, then you'll have to deal with those
(check your /etc/ld.so.conf to be sure it includes the path to the
freetype library or check the php5 fora to see how others have gotten
around the likely 'configure' bug).

Why are you using an old version of the GD library? Just curious.

--Kurt

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Tue Dec  6 19:44:46 2005
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Tue,  6 Dec 2005 19:44:46 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Scope for a Postfix server...
In-Reply-To: <863bl6nlpl.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>
References: <20051206053354.GA3787@patch.com>
	<B0015360141@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
	<863bl6nlpl.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>
Message-ID: <1133927086.43965aae9293b@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

Quoting "Randal L. Schwartz" <merlyn at stonehenge.com>:

> >>>>> "Charles" == Charles Sliger <chaz at bctonline.com> writes:
> 
> Charles> what I meant was that postfix would get the system/domain
> Charles> name via a reverse DNS lookup and then look up that name in a
> Charles> white-list to see if it should be allowed through.
> 
> So your white list contains "comcast.com", and I set up my reverse
> DNS to say "comcast.com"?  Are you going to whitelist me?
> 
> Whitelists like that are trivial to spoof.
> 
> -- 
> Randal L. Schwartz - Stonehenge Consulting Services, Inc. - +1 503 777 0095
> <merlyn at stonehenge.com> <URL:http://www.stonehenge.com/merlyn/>
> Perl/Unix/security consulting, Technical writing, Comedy, etc. etc.
> See PerlTraining.Stonehenge.com for onsite and open-enrollment Perl
> training!
> 
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug
> 
 
As far as spoofing, how do you avoid getting bad dns information?  

By scope, I want to tell postfix explicitly what subnets it can 
deal with externally.  I want to change postfix's default, 
"what I can look up I can see," behavior.  Just because you can 
look up the foo.com smtp server, that doesn't make it valid to 
work with the foo.com site.  Ip filtering rules make smtp 
transactions with foo.com impossible outbound from my mail 
site.  If foo.com connects to my site and my site wants to 
bounce the message foo.com sent to it, there's a major jamming 
problem.  How do I synchronize iptables rules that block smtp
access to all but one external subnet with my postfix 
configuation?  Postfix needs to determine that a server 
connecting to it is from an acceptable subnet.  It needs to 
look up the site name and if the net it gets is not on a 
reachable list, the connection should be terminated immediately.  
I want my mail site to refuse data from other mail sites that 
it cannot connect to because of the local ip filtering rules.
My only other thought is that I need to make changes on the
firewall that my mail site is behind.

Michael C. Robinson

-------------------------------------------------
This mail sent through IMP: http://horde.org/imp/


From chaz at bctonline.com  Wed Dec  7 08:49:18 2005
From: chaz at bctonline.com (Charles Sliger)
Date: Wed, 7 Dec 2005 08:49:18 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Scope for a Postfix server...
In-Reply-To: <1133927086.43965aae9293b@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <B0015392275@bct-mail.bctonline.com>

Why not just block smtp connects from the disallowed subnets in iptables?
That way the disallowed subnets will never be able to contact your smtp
server.
Regards,
Chaz

In-Reply-To: <200512070740.06781.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
References: <43968AE3.4394.A14B9003@localhost>
Message-ID: <4396AA47.11946.A1C62F9D@localhost>

On 7 Dec 2005, at 7:40, Eric Wilhelm wrote:

> Bad package!  Bad!  Somewhere in Settings->Configure Konqueror->File
> Associations, there is a mess of things registered to LyX.  The
> simplest thing would be to click through there and clean them out. 
> The better thing would be to figure out where in the ubuntu package
> the file associations were defined and send a patch to the maintainer.

Thanks. It didn't occur to me that it would be in Konqueror. I found 
the window where I can change the settings. Took me forever to go 
through it because it listed every file type in the known universe. It 
seems Lyx hijacked everything having anything remotely to do with 
text files. Even script files, etc. Unfortunately, when I tried to 
remove Lyx most of the time Konqueror popped up a window 
saying that the service could not be removed. However, I was able 
to demote it and put another app on top so Lyx will now try to open 
the file only if the top application(s) are not available.

Paul suggested where to look for rogue Lyx references and I'll do 
that later. There may be some others in there too, because I can't 
remove certain other services -- noatun, for example. 

> >A related problem is how to select a file in Konqueror without
> >launching it. For some reason it is set to launch with a single
> >click.
> 
> (hold ctrl to just select if you're in single-click mode)

That works. 

> Hmm.  When you first start kde, it asks you about 5 questions.  One of
> these is about "style" and enables this plus about 20 other things.  I
> never had to change it though.   Took some digging to find this.
> 
> Control Center -> Peripherals -> Mouse -> "General" tab  ("Icons"
> section)

Better solution, except I use only Gnome. However, there is an 
equivalent "Configuration Editor" utility. Under "mouse" the "single-
click" box was checked. I unchecked it, but it didn't make any 
difference. Afer unchecking the box I clicked on Apply, but it still 
tries to open or launch the file with a single click. Then I logged out 
and back in, but still no joy. Finally, I shut down the computer and 
restarted it, but a single-click still tries to open or launch the file. 
And checking in the Configuration Editor, the box is still unchecked.

Must be something else that is controlling it. Logically, it must be 
Konqueror overriding the system settings. But I can't find any 
settings for this in Konqueror.

Wait ... I tried something else. I went back to the configuration 
panel for the mouse and right-clicked on the entry for single-click. 
That brought up a context menu where I could edit the key, unseat 
the key, make it the default, or make it mandatory. Choosing Edit 
popped up a small window, but it was grayed out. Oh well. Didn't 
know what to edit there anyway. Left Unseat alone, as I have no 
idea what unseating does. I chose Mandatory and this popped up a 
warning that I was changing something that the Administrator did 
not give me permission to do. But it also said it might not take 
effect until the application was relaunched. I tried shutting down 
and restarting again, but it did not take effect. I think I have to do 
this as root. But that means I have to launch the configuration 
editor as root or with sudo. I don't know what the executable file for 
the Configuration Editor is. There is probably also a config file 
somewhere that I can edit manually from the command line, if I 
knew what and where it was.

Oddly, single-click has always just selected a file when I am in 
other applications, e.g., OpenOffice.org. It's only in Konqueror 
where it tries to open or launch the file.

Thanks for the help so far. Until I get to the bottom of this at least I 
can hold down Ctrl.


From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Dec  7 14:00:55 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 7 Dec 2005 14:00:55 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] DVI, PCI, Linux - found a good card
In-Reply-To: <20051024192417.620F98F03@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
References: <20051024181648.GA13058@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<20051024192417.620F98F03@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
Message-ID: <20051207220055.GA26672@gate.kl-ic.com>


> Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> wrote:
> ---------------------------------------------------------------
> So, the question:  What currently available PCI (not AGP) video
> cards support DVI and Linux?  I would love something with open
> source drivers and super-duper performance, but I will settle
> for binary drivers and 2D functional and cheap.
> ---------------------------------------------------------------
 
On Mon, Oct 24, 2005 at 12:24:12PM -0700, Galen Seitz wrote:
> Here's link where someone compared all of the X drivers.
> http://www.tjansen.de/xfree-features/
> 
> It appears to me that an ATI 9x00 PCI card meets your requirements, though
> the 9250 might require patching X.  Newegg still has a Diamond 9200SE card.
> http://www.newegg.com/Product/Product.asp?Item=N82E16814103155

And finally Keith responds:

I got distracted, and did not make a serious search for a card until
this week.  I am now looking at the display driven by a Compusa Radeon
9250 card - nice and sharp! - using the standard Radeon driver that
came with SUSE 10 .  The card is $60 and 128M, though since all it
will be displaying is 2D graphics that is probably major overkill.

It displays an alternating black/white pixel stipple very nicely.
The old VGA interface made streaks - and more importantly, "m"
characters in xterms, thin vertical lines, etc. used to look bad.

BTW, I hacked the new card in the old fashioned way, by turning off the 
built-in video in the BIOS, then editing /etc/X11/xorg.conf with the
new information for this RV280 device.  There was some worry that
the driver would go looking for an AGP bus card rather than the PCI
card (the SUSE hardware compatability guide only lists the AGP version
of this chipset), but it found it!

So the remaining question - what is the modern way to switch video
cards with SUSE?  Text editing xorg.conf is so nerdish ...

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

***What Is Prefactoring?
You've probably heard of Refactoring (the process of restructuring code
without changing its external behavior), but what is Prefactoring? Ken
Pugh provides the answer, then covers some of the guidelines to
prefactoring in the areas of Extreme Abstraction, Extreme Separation,
and Extreme Readability. Ken is the author of "Prefactoring."
<http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/network/2005/11/15/what-is-prefactoring.html>


***Avoid Common Pitfalls in Greasemonkey 
Mark Pilgrim walks through a major security concern that prompted the
architectural changes in Greasemonkey 0.5, and then provides solutions
to ten common pitfalls to avoid when writing Greasemonkey scripts. The
hacks he describes, as well as the underlying history of the breach in
Greasemonkey 0.3's security, provide a useful lesson on designing secure
user scripts. Mark is the author of "Greasemonkey Hacks."
<http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/network/2005/11/01/avoid-common-greasemonkey-pitfalls.html>

***TCP Tuning and Network Troubleshooting 
Information doesn't travel across networks in one big chunk--it goes in
little packets wrapped in packets wrapped in packets. Sure, you know
that, but did you know that a bit of measuring and a bit of tweaking can
improve your networking performance by two orders of magnitude?
<http://www.onlamp.com/pub/a/onlamp/2005/11/17/tcp_tuning.html>

General caution with SAX:

It has a tendency to always add a PCI_ID mapping for the card to the
xorg.conf.  Can be a problem when duplicating a config across a
machine, but in your case it might be good to remember in case you
want to re-order you cards on the PCI bus some day.

/eli

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Wed Dec  7 23:48:31 2005
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (plug_0 at robinson-west.com)
Date: Wed,  7 Dec 2005 23:48:31 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Scope for a Postfix server...
In-Reply-To: <B0015392275@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
References: <B0015392275@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
Message-ID: <1134028111.4397e54fd47ef@xerxes.robinson-west.com>

   -----------------    -----------     ---------------------
   |Internet client|----| gateway |-----| private mail site |
   -----------------    -----------     ---------------------

Here's what I've done to address the security problem:

Couldn't use easytcp because the version on Redhat 7.3 gateway 
doesn't work with the version on my Fedora Core 1 private mail 
site.

Since I was using samba anyways between the two systems, I chose
to post the valid external ip address using it.

The rules for destination nat that I start up with on my gateway 
point to a dummy ip address, effectively nowhere.

When my private mail site gets a valid external ip address, it 
posts it via samba to the gateway.  The gateway then replaces
the appropriate rules in the prerouting nat table via a bash
script which cron runs every 2 minutes in case an update is
needed.  When the private mail server's firewall changes, the
gateway's firewall changes shortly after.  A daemon would be
nicer, but I don't know how to write daemons.

At first, I didn't think of the fact that masquerading was hiding
the actual source of smtp clients coming into my private mail
server from outside subnets.  This is why the destination nat
rules on the gateway have to be specific.  I had to realize that
whatever gets natted is essentially local traffic at that point.
Is there an alternative to masquerading?  Well, generally the
source is suppose to match the ip net you're on as far as I
know.

This solution seems reasonable.  I'm curious about IPsec or 
secure tunnels as potentially better approaches to this
problem though.  

What I still want to know is if postfix can be programmed
with it's own name resolution scheme that's seperate from what
Linux's /etc/resolv.conf, etc., specifies?  For a mail site 
that should only work with a small list of known hosts plus 
one external host, a hosts file approach seems adequate.  All 
I need to do is cleanly update that special hosts file.

For those who wonder, why Redhat 7.3 on your gateway?  I'm 
using an SMC card in a PLX adapter with hostap.  I don't 
know how to set up an AP using anything newer than Valhalla 
with this equipment.  Also, I do ltsp and nfs root servers
where I am worried about being able to implement all of that
with a more recent Linux distro.

I find with servers that it's easy to move slowly.

-------------------------------------------------
This mail sent through IMP: http://horde.org/imp/

On Thu, Dec 08, 2005 at 07:45:55AM -0800, Piet van Weel wrote:
> Greetings...
> 
> One of the people that I am working with wants me to E-Mail him lots of 
> pictures! Unfortunately I don't want to take the time and man-handle all 
> 111 of them. Anybody think of any snazzy way of scripting this out?
> 
> All of them have the same recipient, the same subject line with the file 
> name attached to the end, and all of them have the same body line.

Easy enough with mutt and bash:
  for file in picture*.jpg ;
  do mutt -a $file -s "This is $file" fred at here.com ;
  done

Optionally, you could use imagemagick or something to do some
processing on the image first, such as increase the compression or
reduce the size.  Pulling EXIF info out and putting it in the email is
left as an exercise for the reader.

On Thu, 8 Dec 2005, John Jordan wrote:

> I bought one of those little shirt-pocket size USB hard disks to 
> make backups of my laptop to. It holds 60 Gb, which is also the 
> same size as the laptop hard disk. 
> 
> When I tried it (with Dump) it got 4 Gb written and then the terminal 
> window gave me an error message that the file was too big. Of 
> course, silly me. When I checked, the drive had been pre-formatted 
> at the factory with Fat32. 
> 
> Since this will be used exclusively with the laptop, which has only 
> Ubuntu on it, I decided I want to reformat with Ext3. But I don't 
> know how to do that. Ubuntu has a dialog box under System > 
> Administration > Disks that has a button for Format, but the button 
> is grayed out for this drive. Strangely it is NOT grayed out for the 
> laptop's drive. Like it won't let me format unused external media, 
> but it WILL let me format the disk containing the whole operating 
> system.
> 
> The drive is mounted as /media/DATASTOR. I tried man format, but 
> got a message "No manual entry for format." I also tried System > 
> Help, only to be reminded that Ubuntu's Help is broken. It always 
> pops up a window that says "The application 'yelp' has quit 
> unexpectedly." I've never been able to see the Help files.
> 
> So where do I find out how to format this new disk?

Find out what the device the drive reports as.  Probably /dev/sda, but 
make sure.  (Should be at the end of dmesg after you plug it in.)

Make sure the drive is not mounted.

Use "fdisk /dev/sda" (or whatever it is called) to change the partition 
type to ext2.  (Just delete the old partition and create a new one.  it 
will automatically be ext2.)

After you save the new partition table, run 'mkfs /dev/sda1' to 
create/format the new file system.

The drive should now mount at a Linux file system.

-- 
#/sbin/fsck /dev/brain
Errors found. Cannot continue. Please run fsck manually without -A option.
Enter root password or hit Control_D to continue.

From ehem at m5p.com  Thu Dec  8 23:17:05 2005
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Thu, 8 Dec 2005 23:17:05 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Format?
In-Reply-To: <20051209012012.DA39B4178@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200512090717.jB97H5AJ027231@m5p.com>

>From: "John Jordan" <johnxj at comcast.net>
> When I tried it (with Dump) it got 4 Gb written and then the terminal 
> window gave me an error message that the file was too big. Of 
> course, silly me. When I checked, the drive had been pre-formatted 
> at the factory with Fat32. 

Apparently the size limitation for ext2 is 2GB.  :-/

You want the -B and -M options for `dump`. These will cause `dump` to
write a series of files, rather than trying to dump everything into one
giant file.

Sample usage might be:

dump -f root.dump-20051208. -B 1048576 -M -j /dev/hda1

Which would produce a series of 1GB bzip-compressed files named
"root.dump-20051208.001", "root.dump-20051208.002", etc. Thus bypassing
any filesystem size limitations.



As for placing an ext2/3 filesystem on a device, `mkfs`. And as has been
mentioned, MAKE SURE IT ISN'T MOUNTED. At best if you forget this, the
disk will end up with a messed up filesystem. At worst you'll end up with
a screwed up filesystem, and your system will reboot (or worse, *won't*
reboot) because you confused the kernel.

As to the step involved, `fdisk` is optional. You could simply do
`mkfs -t ext2 -T largefile /dev/sda1`. The mismatch from the partition
table may confuse some utilities, or other OSes that expect the type in
the partition table to be accurate though.

Even better, if you're only placing on giant filesystem on it, you could
simply do `mkfs -t ext2 -T largefile /dev/sda`. The -T largefile tells
it to create fewer i-nodes, saves some space by limiting the maximum
number of possible files. Given that you're planning to use this for
backups, for which 256MB is *small*, this is appropriate.


>From: "John Jordan" <johnxj at comcast.net>
> OK, thanks to you and Alan. But I just got a notice that PDXLUG is 
> having their meeting tonight at the Fireside. I usually go to those 
> anyway. I'll just haul the stuff down there and surely someone can 
> teach me how to do this.

They'll show you the steps we're talking about. This is safer as you'll
have someone to make sure you don't mess this up (these tools can do
severe damage if used correctly).

Subject: [PLUG] Format?

On Thu, 2005-12-08 at 23:17 -0800, Elliott Mitchell wrote:
> >From: "John Jordan" <johnxj at comcast.net>
> > When I tried it (with Dump) it got 4 Gb written and then the terminal 
> > window gave me an error message that the file was too big. Of 
> > course, silly me. When I checked, the drive had been pre-formatted 
> > at the factory with Fat32. 
> 
> Apparently the size limitation for ext2 is 2GB.  :-/

I have created larger than 2gig files on ext2.  (DVD ISOs are larger.)
You have to be running a 2.6 kernel though.

What kernel are you running?


> 
> You want the -B and -M options for `dump`. These will cause `dump` to
> write a series of files, rather than trying to dump everything into one
> giant file.
> 
> Sample usage might be:
> 
> dump -f root.dump-20051208. -B 1048576 -M -j /dev/hda1
> 
> Which would produce a series of 1GB bzip-compressed files named
> "root.dump-20051208.001", "root.dump-20051208.002", etc. Thus bypassing
> any filesystem size limitations.
> 
> 
> 
> As for placing an ext2/3 filesystem on a device, `mkfs`. And as has been
> mentioned, MAKE SURE IT ISN'T MOUNTED. At best if you forget this, the
> disk will end up with a messed up filesystem. At worst you'll end up with
> a screwed up filesystem, and your system will reboot (or worse, *won't*
> reboot) because you confused the kernel.
> 
> As to the step involved, `fdisk` is optional. You could simply do
> `mkfs -t ext2 -T largefile /dev/sda1`. The mismatch from the partition
> table may confuse some utilities, or other OSes that expect the type in
> the partition table to be accurate though.
> 
> Even better, if you're only placing on giant filesystem on it, you could
> simply do `mkfs -t ext2 -T largefile /dev/sda`. The -T largefile tells
> it to create fewer i-nodes, saves some space by limiting the maximum
> number of possible files. Given that you're planning to use this for
> backups, for which 256MB is *small*, this is appropriate.
> 
> 
> >From: "John Jordan" <johnxj at comcast.net>
> > OK, thanks to you and Alan. But I just got a notice that PDXLUG is 
> > having their meeting tonight at the Fireside. I usually go to those

> 
> They'll show you the steps we're talking about. This is safer as you'll
> have someone to make sure you don't mess this up (these tools can do
> severe damage if used correctly).
>

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sat Dec 10 11:04:54 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sat, 10 Dec 2005 11:04:54 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Just Say No to Microsoft by Tony Bove
Message-ID: <20051210190454.GA2360@gate.kl-ic.com>


I encountered a semi-nifty book, "Just Say No To Microsoft" by Tony
Bove, No Starch Press, $16.47 from Amazon .  I plan to buy half a dozen
copies for Christmas presents.  It isn't perfect, but it will help
the recipients and save me some time.

About half the verbiage in the book deals with why Microsoft is bad.
The first section of the book concentrates on the history of personal
computing, and is more diatribe than most of the people I am giving
this to will want.  However, the other sections are mostly about how
to access alternatives.  The book is friendly to Linux, but it is
mostly a love letter to the Macintosh, why that is the best alternative
for most people, and how you can maximize your open-source experience
on that platform.  It is not blindly pro-Mac;  where Apple strays 
towards the dark side, Bove is quick to point it out. 

There are a few factual errors - Bove has Linux Torvalds hailing from
Norway, for example - but these do not get in the way of the primary
function, reducing dependence on proprietary Microsoft monoculture.
Until a less polemic, more cookbook-oriented book comes around, this
will be pretty handy for teaching people how to minimize the damage
that their Microsoft addiction causes.

And that is how it helps me.  I don't know how many of you are 
frequently yanked out of your cozy Linux world to clean up the
Microsoft messes of others, but it happens a lot to me.  I am
considered the "computer fixit" guy by a lot of relatives.  I am
always resisting the urge to grab these people by the shoulders and
shake, while screaming "You idiot!  YOU MORON!  You brought this on
yourself by running this Windows CRAP!  I can't keep fixing your 
computer messes if you don't fix the reason your have them!  Change
to Linux!  Buy a Mac!  Stop screwing up your life and mine!!!!"

So now I can hand them a book and let it do the screaming for me.
While it is more diatribe than most readers will like, it is not as
much as their sloppy computing habits have earned them ... 

Keith

PS - one of the pleasures of the book for me was Bove's statement
that a large part of Apple's success is its connection to the music
community, partly due to early adopters like musician Herbie Hancock.
<brag>
I introduced Herbie to the Apple II, which I used as the controller
for an electronic switch for his analog synthesizer keyboards.  I
presented the reasons to use an Apple (and the alternative, a mini-
LSI11) during a break in the recording of "Monster" at United
Western Studios.  Producer David Rubison, sound engineer Fred Catero,
and a half dozen other musicians were listening in.  They liked the
pitch so much that they decided they wanted Apple II computers (fully
laden) too - I ended up with a lap full of American Express Gold
Cards.   Herbie's keyboard manager Bryan Bell and I trundled down
to the Santa Monica Computer Store (the only place you could buy
Apples in the LA area in 1980) and bought the place out.  "We'll
take 8 of those, 16 of those (do you have any bigger monitors?), we
want them NOW."  Bryan was very persuasive.  The store had the usual
(for the time) collection of teenage hackers pounding away on the
display units - some of them were unhappy when those units were
taken away on the spot, to be repackaged and sold to us.

Herbie got his Apple and his keyboard switch, though the analog
synthesizer technology was soon replaced by digital synthesizers and
MIDI.  However, he spent most of the next year glued to his Apple II
and his modem, BBS'ing with 14 year old hackers about computer
programming.  He loves math and science, and sometime I can tell
the story about our trip to Edwards to watch the first space shuttle
landing.  Maybe we can bring him in for an Advanced Topics talk.
</brag>

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

I was reading on Ubuntu Forums and found a thread about making 
backups. Someone suggested Kdar. I installed it via Synaptic on 
my Ubuntu-64 Breezy laptop. I launched it from terminal because it 
did not create a launch setting under the Applications menu. The 
Help files were not working, so I fumbled my way through starting a 
new archive. The archive was to be named 12_10_2005 on my 
external USB disk, which is formatted ext3.

It ran for about 50-60 seconds and then locked up Linux. Keyboard 
and mouse dead. Had to hit the power button. 

When I restarted I tried again, this time with no other applications 
running. It seemed to go much better. It progressed to about 150 
Mb, about 10 minutes running time, then suddenly it disappeared 
from the screen. I had left the terminal window open and the last 
line was that it had crashed.

I wanted to see if it had actually created part of the archive, so I 
launched Konqueror. This produced an error message that KDE 
could not run. (I use Gnome for desktop.) I tried a few things, but 
nothing would get Konqueror to launch. At that point I decided to 
shut down and restart. I did System > Shut down and it appeared 
to shut down normally.

It restarted normally and gave me the usual login screen. But when 
I entered my username and password it gave me an error message 
that my profile "is not installed on this computer." I tried several 
other options under "Session" but the only one that will log me in is 
"Failsafe Terminal."

Supposedly, this is a way for me to fix wat is wrong. Unfortunately, 
I have no clue as to what needs to be fixed or how to do it from a 
terminal. The terminal window is running in a corner of the screen, 
and it appears that X is running.

At this time my computer is essentially dead. The only thing I can 
think of to do is to wipe the hard disk and reinstall from scratch. 
Ugh. I do have a backup that I made with Dump a couple days ago. 
The backup file is intact on the external USB drive -- at least it 
should be still there, unless that Kdar POS buggered it up 
somehow. It is a backup from /, so it should be the whole shebang 
(9+ Gb file). I have no idea how to use it to restore the system, 
however.

I'm in deep doo-doo here. :(

On Sat, 10 Dec 2005, John Jordan wrote:

> At this time my computer is essentially dead. The only thing I can think of
> to do is to wipe the hard disk and reinstall from scratch.

   That's the Microsoft solution. You can apparently access the system in
single-user mode as root. That's all you need.

> I'm in deep doo-doo here. :(

   Yeah, we've all done something like this a time or three.

   I'm completely unfamiliar with Ubuntu and Debian, but I assume you're using
GRUB as the bootloader. You can pass it the location of the boot partition
and you can edit /etc/password (for example, by assigning your username a new
password).

   The SysAdmin gurus on this list can give you the step-by-step instructions
on what to do. I'd go to my bookcase for Carla's book (or one of the other
references I keep handy) and check how to recover from a bad system crash.
It's not impossible, just time consuming.

First, in the there have been problems with USB external drives and
Linux.  I found a chipset dependence, but did not have time to pursue
it.  At the time, with both late 2.4.XX kernels and early 2.6.X kernels,
the process would go for a while and then hang.  I do not know what the
status of the problem is now.  If it still is a problem, then some
careful attention and tracing would probably provide enough detail that
the USB driver folks could take it from there.  I suggest loaning the
drive to somebody else, and see if they see similar problems.  That's
why we're here, guy!

Second, from some of the other problems you have been having, it is 
possible that you have flaky main system hardware.  One of the things
the World Needs is a good hardware test suite.  With all the different
kinds of hardware out there, that would be an enormous task.  So
generally we are stuck with swapping the hardware around and seeing
what it works on.  

Question - did you buy any of the hardware pre"Fry"ed?

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Sat Dec 10 16:44:04 2005

	<c29537840512101548m3ebe794dhc165271cb3e3303a@mail.gmail.com>
	<439AFC2B.991.B2A61172@localhost>
Message-ID: <c29537840512101644p2214683fla4eadfa28cac4124@mail.gmail.com>

On 12/10/05, John Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> On 10 Dec 2005, at 15:48, Jason R. Martin wrote:
>
> > Did you mount the filesystem?
>
> Ummm, no. But I didn't mount the external USB drive either, and it
> saw that fine. I just assumed that it could not see the computer's
> hard drive because a Live CD is not supposed to touch the
> computer's hard disk.
>
> But I could try the Live CD again and give it a whirl. I know about
> "mount" and "umount," but there are things you have to put after
> the command, like *what* you want to mount. I don't know that
> syntax.
>
> Bear in mind that the Live CD does show a filesystem. It's the
> filesystem from the CD, not the filesystem on the computer.

Right, you can see the CD filesystem because it has to be mounted to
use the Live system.  It sees the USB filesystem because it is
configured to look for hot-swappable filesystems like USB thumbdrives.
 It doesn't automatically mount your hard drive filesystem, because
normally those are mounted "manually" by the /etc/fstab file.

mount syntax in it's simplest form is just "mount <device> <mount
point>".  So try this:

# fdisk -l /dev/hda  # This will list the partitions on your hard drive
# mkdir /tmp/my-hard-drive  # This will create a temporary mount point
# mount /dev/hda1 /tmp/my-hard-drive  # This will mount it

I expect you want /dev/hda1, but check the output of fdisk first.

And now it's time to watch Illinois cream the poor Ducks.  I love
Illinois basketball.

Jason

On 10 Dec 2005, at 15:45, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> First, in the there have been problems with USB external drives and
> Linux.  I found a chipset dependence, but did not have time to pursue
> it.  At the time, with both late 2.4.XX kernels and early 2.6.X
> kernels, the process would go for a while and then hang.  I do not
> know what the status of the problem is now.  If it still is a problem,
> then some careful attention and tracing would probably provide enough
> detail that the USB driver folks could take it from there.  I suggest
> loaning the drive to somebody else, and see if they see similar
> problems.  That's why we're here, guy!
 
On Sat, Dec 10, 2005 at 03:58:17PM -0800, John Jordan wrote:
> The Live CD sees the external USB drive just fine. The archive file 
> made with Dump two days ago is still there -- 9 Gb. And Dump had 
> no problem writing to this USB drive. So I seriously doubt there is a 
> hardware or driver problem with the new USB drive.

I was unclear.  The problem is low rep-rate and hard to reproduce - I
would see it about once per 10GB moved, sometimes after a few hundred
megabytes and sometimes after 50GB.  It seems to relate to
synchronization and error recovery.  You can find a writeup at
http://www.keithl.com/usb2bug.html , and mentions of it in the PLUG
archives and elsewhere.  If you have repeatedly filled and emptied your
external drive without error, then you can rule out hardware errors.


> plastic wrappings. I don't think this is a hardware problem.
> 
> In fact, other than flaky software, I have had no problems at all with 
> this computer. This problem was created by Kdar. No question 
> about it. 

But without the actual bug detected or at least characterized, how can
you tell?  You may have no question about it, but the people you expect
to solve the problem for you do have questions.  Questions are how 
answers are found.   You have learned that you cannot make Kdar work
for you using the thought and skills you are willing to apply.  That
does not necessarily mean the code is broken, but it does mean it is
not suited for you.

As a person that designs hardware, let me assure you that none of it
is perfect.  "New in the plastic wrap" means somebody knows how to
put a unit in a bag and seal it in a box.  So do the clowns at Fry's,
and they sell "defective new in the box" stuff all the time.  Even
the best hardware from unimpeachable sources comes with failure
rates approaching a percent.  Sometimes every instance of a product
is defective, and the manufacturer sells the dreck anyway.

I suspect software is indeed the problem, but the process of fault
isolation requires slicing the problem into pieces, and the 
hardware/software slice is usually good for cutting a problem in
half.  However, intermittent software problems that behave like
you are describing typically have their roots in infrequent conditions
in hardware, which should be detected and bypassed and are not.
Since the problem is locking up your machine, it is likely something
down in the kernel or drivers or below - it is very unlikely that
any user space code, even code run by root, can cause a machine to
lock up without help from the kernel and the hardware.

> I think that the best solution right now is to use that Dump archive 
> file to restore. Unfortunately, I have no idea how to do that. I 
> googled and found some instructions for Dump/Restore, but they 
> were written in Geekian. I understood one or two words per 
> sentence, usually things like "the" and "an." :(

And geekian - or books, or something else that requires an investment
of effort, is how you are going to find a solution.  Dump is not as
hard as most things in Linux, and it has been around long enough that
you can find instructions in 30 year old books.  I can dump my usr
partition with something like  "dump -0f dumpfile /usr" - that is 
about as easy as it gets.  File maintenance, and restores, require
quite a bit more effort, which is one reason why I do not like the
apparent simplicity of dump.

I no longer use dump, and maintain an open source disk-to-disk backup
program, dirvish.  However, dirvish is a lot more complicated and 
powerful than dump, so I would not suggest it in your case.  It does
mean I have seen a LOT of disk-related and USB-related errors from
dirvish users, which is why I believe that intermittent hardware 
failure, or kernel code that does not bypass those failures, is a
distinct possibility in your case.

> Failing that, I could just reinstall from the Ubuntu-64 Install disk. 
> But that will wipe out everything, I think. Lots of work to put 
> everything back. I tried the Install disk just now, but it doesn't seem 
> to have a Repair option.

A lot of the questions you ask are answered by some good books.  I 
suggest Carla's "Linux Cookbook", Peter Harrison's "Linux Quick Fix
Guide", and the "Linux Administration Handbook" by Nemeth.  These are
not self-licking ice cream cones, however;  the sweetness will require
some effort to get access to.

Linux for the desktop is still arriving.  It has not become effort-free
to use yet, though it is getting closer daily.  If you want something
closer to effort-free, you may want to consider a Mac.  Linux is (IMHO)
easier to use than Windows, in the sense that the sum of easy and hard
things necessary to safely operate a computer are easier as a whole for
Linux (try doing a bare-metal disk restore from backups in Windows XP). 
But for now Macs are easier still, and allow you to substitute money
for effort.  If you can't afford either money or effort, then you will
have many disappointments.

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

To make sure its a problem with your account, you can create a new
account (via adduser/useradd) and see if you can log in to that
account.  If that works, then move every file in your home directory
that starts with a dot somewhere else and then try logging in.  If
that works then you know something went wrong with your dotfiles
(where personal account settings are stored) and you can decide
whether to try moving them back and fixing it or just start with a
reset set of settings.

> I could su and then create a new account for myself, or reset jjj's
> password, except that I already tried that and the Live CD doesn't
> seem to allow becoming root. E.g., from a terminal I did 'su' and
> when it asked for the password I gave it my root password. It
> wouldn't take it. Of course it would not. The Live CD has no idea
> who I am or who root is on this computer. On the other hand, the
> commands the fellow on the Ubuntu forums gave me all started
> with sudo and they all worked, without even asking for a password.
> I don't understand that.

Ubuntu has a different philosophy than tradition *nix does, choosing
to disable the root account and add NOPASSWD sudo privileges to the
first account created during installation.  Look here:

http://www.ubuntulinux.org/support/documentation/faq/root
