From ehem at m5p.com  Sat Dec 10 20:35:13 2005
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Sat, 10 Dec 2005 20:35:13 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] HELP! Broken Ubuntu! Can't login!
In-Reply-To: <20051211000205.0604141C2@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200512110435.jBB4ZDCi046453@m5p.com>

>From: "John Jordan" <johnxj at comcast.net>
> I think that the best solution right now is to use that Dump archive 
> file to restore. Unfortunately, I have no idea how to do that. I 
> googled and found some instructions for Dump/Restore, but they 
> were written in Geekian. I understood one or two words per 
> sentence, usually things like "the" and "an." :(

Though imperfect, the man page for restore is quite useful. Comes down
to two steps:

First, prepare the restore target. `mkfs` as mentioned previously. For a
regular filesystem you might want to use -j, and get ext3's journaling
capability. Likely your disk is /dev/hda1.

Second, do the restore. Mount the filesystem (`mkdir /mnt/target; mount
-t ext2/3 /dev/hda1 /mnt/target` will do). Then go into that directory
and run restore, `restore -r -f <archive>`. If you used -M during dump
you'll use it here too. -j -z and -B will be figured by restore from the
archive. Do the level 0/full first, followed by incrementals in
increasing order (I doubt you've got incrementals, so don't worry about
this). Once this is done you'll have a complete image of what you had
before. At this point remove the file "restoresymtab", this is a leftover
from how restore works. You're done, you can unmount the filesystem, or
proceed to other things.

You'll need a third step though, restoration of the boot loader. Alas I
haven't done this for GRUB. I'm unsure whether Ubuntu's setup will allow
it, but it may be possible to reboot with the live CD in the drive. It
may find your restored filesystem, and boot your system the way it was;
in this case it might be as simple as running `grub` at a root shell.


Note for the elders. The Linux dump is a SysV-style, rather than the
ancient awful BSD-style dump. SysV-style restores via the normal API,
rather than directly accessing the target filesystem via block device.
This has advantages of not needing the target to be identical in every
way (notably size), in fact you can restore onto a completely different
type of filesystem.

You're seeing a multi-threaded application. Each thread is assigned 
its own process ID, but in actuality they share memory space.

From ehem at m5p.com  Sun Dec 11 14:17:32 2005
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Sun, 11 Dec 2005 14:17:32 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Memory usage in top with multiple processes
In-Reply-To: <20051211200004.D6FD0419E@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200512112217.jBBMHWDH054452@m5p.com>

>From: Matt Alexander <lowbassman at gmail.com>
> Here's something I've always wondered...  When I run "top" I may see 
> quite a few of the same process name listed.  Each entry has the exact 
> same values from SIZE, RSS, and SHARE.  However, multiplying the number 
> of entries by the RSS value would far exceed the amount of RAM + swap on 
> the box.  So...  what's really going on?

As Paul mentioned, if they're all identical than most likely they're
either threads or forks which are all sharing the same memory.

SHARE may include files mmap()ed by multiple processes as well as SysV
shared memory, and the former can be written to disk rather than swap.
I'm unsure whether top will include memory sharing caused by threads and
forks in this number. Crucially though, this will appear in multiple
processes (worse some of those might have differing portions mapped, and
so even if the number differs for two processes they could be sharing).
This is why top gives you a separate number for shared memory.

RSS is the portion of the process's address space that is actively
mapped (been used "recently"), not something excluding SIZE. Therefore
you shouldn't be including this in your total.

SIZE can include a number of extras. Notably any "file" that has been
mmap()ed. Though mmap()ed files may take up memory, they will never take
swap space because they're tied to an actual file on disk (FreeBSD though
may cache these in swap though). Of greater note is that X will mmap()
the framebuffer, and that will be included in SIZE. I suspect nVIDIA's
driver may also give framebuffer access to programs if they can access
the appropriate device.

From ehem at m5p.com  Mon Dec 12 13:10:12 2005
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Mon, 12 Dec 2005 13:10:12 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] HELP! Broken Ubuntu! Can't login!
In-Reply-To: <20051212200008.297AA427A@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200512122110.jBCLAC1s064619@m5p.com>

>From: "John Jordan" <johnxj at comcast.net>
> Unfortunately, the instructions for Restore that I have found are less 
> than clear. For example, they talk about "target" as though I know 
> what that means. Is that the folder where I give the command? Or 
> the folder where the files are to be restored to? And what about 
> subfolders? I haven't found the answers in the online documentation 
> or the man page. And lots of other terms. The syntax for the 
> Restore command is pretty intensive -- tons of choices. Assuming I 
> can figure it out, I still don't know what folder I'm supposed to be in 
> when I give the command. 

"target" will 99% of the time refer to the destination filesystem.

You'll want to be in the target/destination directory when you run
restore. Once there you need the "-r" ("restore") option.

Simple command sequence would be:

cd <target> (or destination, take your prefered terminology)
restore -r -f <dumpfile>
rm restoresymtab

This assumes your target/destination has already been prepared (mkfs and
mounted). As you'll note from the sequence, you're in the
target/destination directory when you run `restore`, and therefore you
give a full path to the "-f" option.

On 12 Dec 2005, at 13:10, Elliott Mitchell wrote:

> "target" will 99% of the time refer to the destination filesystem.
> 
> You'll want to be in the target/destination directory when you run
> restore. Once there you need the "-r" ("restore") option.
> 
> Simple command sequence would be:
> 
> cd <target> (or destination, take your prefered terminology)
> restore -r -f <dumpfile>
> rm restoresymtab
> 
> This assumes your target/destination has already been prepared (mkfs
> and mounted). As you'll note from the sequence, you're in the
> target/destination directory when you run `restore`, and therefore you
> give a full path to the "-f" option.

From ehem at m5p.com  Mon Dec 12 18:06:39 2005
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Mon, 12 Dec 2005 18:06:39 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] HELP! Broken Ubuntu! Can't login!
In-Reply-To: <20051213001153.8373544F3@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200512130206.jBD26dFG066858@m5p.com>

>From: "John Jordan" <johnxj at comcast.net>
> And along with that, is there a command like "cloneuser"? 
> Considering that a new user is virgin, I'd like to clone myself. (Hey, 
> not a bad idea in the real world, too!)

I thought there was an option to adduser, but I'm not seeing it.

> On 12 Dec 2005, at 13:10, Elliott Mitchell wrote:
> > This assumes your target/destination has already been prepared (mkfs
> > and mounted). As you'll note from the sequence, you're in the
> > target/destination directory when you run `restore`, and therefore you
> > give a full path to the "-f" option.
> 
> OK, so that means Linux has to be running, because Restore is a 
> Linux program, right? So if (god forbid) my computer is stolen or 
> destroyed, but I have a Dump file on the pocket drive sitting at 
> home, I could restore from the dump file, but first I'd have to get a 
> new computer and install Linux on it. Does the Dump file then 
> replace whatever Linux is installed? Sounds like there would be 
> bits and pieces of the new Linux installation left over -- doesn't 
> sound like a good thing. But I have probably misunderstood the 
> procedure.

All backup solutions have the issue of how much do you need to recreate
before restoring (the "bootstrap" problem). In all cases you'll need to
(re)create the parition table (fdisk), and create new empty filesystems
(mkfs). Some restoration programs may help you with those though. Booting
off an emergency CD is generally sufficient, you don't need to go through
a full installation (unless your backups are on CDs and you don't have
multiple drives).

Once you've got empty filesystem(s) and access to the backup, both `tar`
and `restore` will recreate everything else from there. Settings, kernel,
everything. Often followed by a step to reload the bootloader. Of major
note, neither requires much recreated prior to restoration, they only
need disks operational and a text console; no sound nor graphics.


> But speaking of Dump and backups in general, I did some 
> searching this morning on Ubuntu forums for recommended backup 
> utiities. Dump was mentioned only once, and rather negatively. 

Yes that is typical nowadays due to the older BSD-style restore program
which really was nasty. Thankfully, the ext2 restore program is the newer
SysV-style which is quite nice. In general most of the criticisms of
dump fall into one of two categories: First, criticisms which only apply
to BSD-style dump. Second, criticisms which apply equally to all backup
programs.


Until a reviewer has actually tried some flavor of SysV-style restore
(both ext2's dump/restore and SunOS's ufsdump/ufsrestore are in this
category), I feel I can legitimately ignore the writer's criticisms. I'm
well aware BSD-style restore is *nasty*, SysV-style isn't.

The criticisms of looking directly at the filesystem when the kernel may
well be modifying it apply just as well to tar. This exact same issue
applies to all backup solutions, just the manifestation differs. For tar,
if a process is writing to a file during the time the backup is running,
you'll lose the file. In what way is dump inferior here? `rsync` suffers
the exact same downfalls.

OTOH dump/restore have the crucial advantage that you'll save every file,
every link, and do so efficiently. Crucially you'll catch files that get
renamed, saving *only* the link, not the entire file contents. In order
to get a similar effect, GNU tar has to keep a hash of visited i-nodes;
this works, but isn't as efficient as dump, and could be fooled by
filesystem characteristics. Additionally, dump will deal with sparse
files very efficiently, other programs have to read the entire file.


I've yet to see a credible reason to not use ext2's dump program. While
I see plenty of reasons to use it over other backup programs (at least
for backups to archives).

As to the easy backup program you were refering to...  Sounds like it is
using a graphical desktop during backup. This *really* scares me because
it might also need that for restoration (gee rebuild your X config in
just to restore from bare metal, ouch!).

Thanks for a long story explaining some things to me in a manner 
that I actually understood (sort of). I did have to reinstall Windows 
2000 once when a hard disk went to hard disk heaven. Umm, wait 
... that disk prolly went to hard disk hell, since it was warranted for 
five years and lasted two. I understand what you said about how 
much you have to rebuild before you can restore. At the time I was 
using Backup Exec, written by the same people who wrote the 
basic Windows 2000 backup utility -- same thing but with more 
bells and whistles. Once I had a new hard disk I had to reinstall a 
basic Windows 2000 system, install Backup Exec, then do the 
restore. However, to its credit, it completely overwrote the basic 
system I had just installed and left me with the original system 
completely intact, even down to what service packs had been 
applied.

So I understand that having to set up the filesystems before doing 
a restore is trivial, really. And it hadn't occurred to me that I could 
do the restore with a live CD. (Nothing like that in WinWorld.) 
Except that, having just gone through an nerve-wracking problem 
with a broken user profile, I discovered that the live CDs are 
generally written to prevent any attempt to touch the filesystem. 
That's the whole point of the live CD -- "try it out, it won't touch your 
existing setup."

Unless I stumble across a better alternative, for the time being I am 
going to continue to use Dump. The Simple Backup Solution is just 
not ready for prime time, although I suppose I'll probably go to it 
eventually -- I like stuff that is graphical and easy. Still, Dump is 
easy for me to use also. I have the command line syntax to do it 
and it works. I can create a new backup as often as I want, and 
then delete the older ones as space on the USB disk gets 
cramped.

However, if I need to restore from a Dump file, you'll hear me in 
here screaming HELP! Oh well. It's for emergencies, right? 
Hopefully that will never happen. Or at least not until I graduate 
from n00b status. This stuff is always learned the hard way. :(

From lowbassman at gmail.com  Mon Dec 12 22:22:30 2005
From: lowbassman at gmail.com (Matt Alexander)
Date: Mon, 12 Dec 2005 23:22:30 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Memory usage in top with multiple processes
In-Reply-To: <200512112217.jBBMHWDH054452@m5p.com>
References: <200512112217.jBBMHWDH054452@m5p.com>
Message-ID: <439E68A6.2080503@gmail.com>

Elliott Mitchell wrote:
>> From: Matt Alexander <lowbassman at gmail.com>
>> Here's something I've always wondered...  When I run "top" I may see 
>> quite a few of the same process name listed.  Each entry has the exact 
>> same values from SIZE, RSS, and SHARE.  However, multiplying the number 
>> of entries by the RSS value would far exceed the amount of RAM + swap on 
>> the box.  So...  what's really going on?
>>     
>
> As Paul mentioned, if they're all identical than most likely they're
> either threads or forks which are all sharing the same memory.
>
> SHARE may include files mmap()ed by multiple processes as well as SysV
> shared memory, and the former can be written to disk rather than swap.
> I'm unsure whether top will include memory sharing caused by threads and
> forks in this number. Crucially though, this will appear in multiple
> processes (worse some of those might have differing portions mapped, and
> so even if the number differs for two processes they could be sharing).
> This is why top gives you a separate number for shared memory.
>
> RSS is the portion of the process's address space that is actively
> mapped (been used "recently"), not something excluding SIZE. Therefore
> you shouldn't be including this in your total.
>
> SIZE can include a number of extras. Notably any "file" that has been
> mmap()ed. Though mmap()ed files may take up memory, they will never take
> swap space because they're tied to an actual file on disk (FreeBSD though
> may cache these in swap though). Of greater note is that X will mmap()
> the framebuffer, and that will be included in SIZE. I suspect nVIDIA's
> driver may also give framebuffer access to programs if they can access
> the appropriate device.
>
>
>   
Thank you Elliott and Paul for the explanations.  Memory usage on Linux 
is a little less mysterious to me now.  :-)
~M

John,

Any way you can perform a restore on another system to be familiar with
Dumps mechanisms? I hate to think that you will wait for a crisis to
find out how it works. I cringed when you wrote: "Hopefully that will
never happen." 

Remember: Whatever CAN go wrong, WILL go wrong. 

It is inevitable that you will need to do a restore. Perhaps you can
take a dump file and redirect it to another piece of similar hardware? 

I only say this because I have been totally burned before by not
familiarizing myself with the restore process until the situation is
critical. 

BTW: That's a great point using a live CD for restore purposes. I will
try that out this week. 

David

-----Original Message-----
From: John Jordan [mailto:johnxj at comcast.net] 
Sent: Monday, December 12, 2005 9:52 PM
To: General Linux/UNIX discussion and help;civil and on-topic
Subject: [PLUG] Linux backup issues for dumbass noobies

On 12 Dec 2005, at 18:06, Elliott Mitchell wrote:

> As to the easy backup program you were refering to...  Sounds like it 
> is using a graphical desktop during backup. This *really* scares me 
> because it might also need that for restoration (gee rebuild your X 
> config in just to restore from bare metal, ouch!).

<Changed subject since the login emergency is now history>

Thanks for a long story explaining some things to me in a manner that I
actually understood (sort of). I did have to reinstall Windows 2000 once
when a hard disk went to hard disk heaven. Umm, wait ... that disk
prolly went to hard disk hell, since it was warranted for five years and
lasted two. I understand what you said about how much you have to
rebuild before you can restore. At the time I was using Backup Exec,
written by the same people who wrote the basic Windows 2000 backup
utility -- same thing but with more bells and whistles. Once I had a new
hard disk I had to reinstall a basic Windows 2000 system, install Backup
Exec, then do the restore. However, to its credit, it completely
overwrote the basic system I had just installed and left me with the
original system completely intact, even down to what service packs had
been applied.

So I understand that having to set up the filesystems before doing a
restore is trivial, really. And it hadn't occurred to me that I could do
the restore with a live CD. (Nothing like that in WinWorld.) Except
that, having just gone through an nerve-wracking problem with a broken
user profile, I discovered that the live CDs are generally written to
prevent any attempt to touch the filesystem. 
That's the whole point of the live CD -- "try it out, it won't touch
your existing setup."

Unless I stumble across a better alternative, for the time being I am
going to continue to use Dump. The Simple Backup Solution is just not
ready for prime time, although I suppose I'll probably go to it
eventually -- I like stuff that is graphical and easy. Still, Dump is
easy for me to use also. I have the command line syntax to do it and it
works. I can create a new backup as often as I want, and then delete the
older ones as space on the USB disk gets cramped.

However, if I need to restore from a Dump file, you'll hear me in here
screaming HELP! Oh well. It's for emergencies, right? 
Hopefully that will never happen. Or at least not until I graduate from
n00b status. This stuff is always learned the hard way. :(

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Tue Dec 13 11:58:16 2005
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Tue, 13 Dec 2005 11:58:16 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] DSL
In-Reply-To: <200512131144.11948.bakilpatrick@oregon-net.com>
References: <200512131144.11948.bakilpatrick@oregon-net.com>

# from Bruce Kilpatrick
# on Tuesday 13 December 2005 11:44 am:

>The Verizon web site doesn't say anything about Linux.

Get an ISP that has heard of Linux?  (Anyone heard of one?)

>?We will be ordering the router that is offered with the package.

You probably don't need (or want) whatever windows software they send 
you.  Too bad the CD's don't make better coasters (not absorbant enough 
and that hole in the middle leaks), but mine seems to do well enough 
for setting a coffee mug down temporarily.

If their router is at all decent, you can just login to 192.168.1.1 or 
whatever with you browser and the password in the book (change it to 
something else (and don't lose it!))  Configure all of the clicky 
things until they look right and make sure that it says it is connected 
when the phone line is plugged into it.

Then you just have to setup all of your boxen to get dhcp or at least be 
on the same subnet as the router (and if you're using static IP's, 
you'll need to enter the router's address by hand as the "default 
gateway".)  At that point, you should all be masqueraded onto the 
internets via the router's single public IP.

--Eric

Message-ID: <c834ec80512131408o49795470g1416633cd771d310@mail.gmail.com>

On 12/13/05, dan at fiddlers-green.info <dan at fiddlers-green.info> wrote:
> All,
>
> I'm setting up cheap multi-zone audio using 4 seperate linux boxes with sounds
> cards around my house. I have a central server with my ogg vorbis files that
> I'll share out to each of the audio boxes. My question is should I use samba or
> nfs, and why one over the other?

It may be simpler to stream the audio with something like icecast, and
have each client machine play from the stream rather than a file. 
This, of course isn't without tradeoffs -- you'll have synchronized
audio everwhere, but changing from one song to another is not as easy.

I used to have DSL through DSL-Only in Verizon land. If you order the 
line through DSL-Only, they handle all the Verizon stuff. You get to pay 
two bills this way, but the ISP will admit to knowing that Linux exists, 
and it works.

I'd still be with them, except when I bought my house, 4000 feet from 
the CO, Verizon installed my phone line on a digital loop. That makes it 
incompatible with DSL. They refused to re-engineer the line, even though 
that would mean I would pay them more. So I switched to Speakeasy's 
one-link service, which is DSL on a dry pair... No dial tone required. 
Then I got VoIP and told Verizon to turn off their phone line. I didn't 
need it anymore. So they completely lost a dialtone customer because 
they wouldn't give me what I wanted.

Anyway, if you can, and Verizon won't admit that Linux exists, I'd say 
get a different ISP. Keep in mind that the last time I did this, you had 
to order the service through the ISP, or Verizon would assume that you 
want them to be both the ISP and the line provider.

Russ


From linux at frankhunt.com  Tue Dec 13 14:14:35 2005
From: linux at frankhunt.com (fh hillsboro)
Date: Tue, 13 Dec 2005 14:14:35 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] nfs v. samba
In-Reply-To: <1134509400.439f3d582c612@webmail.fiddlers-green.info>
References: <1134509400.439f3d582c612@webmail.fiddlers-green.info>
Message-ID: <439F47CB.1000306@frankhunt.com>

I'd probably go with nfs just because smb tends to be a bit more 
"chatty".  If you have any plans for Windoze systems in the future, then 
go with samba because all Win systems speak it.  You might also want to 
consider that one of the reasons that smb is more verbose is that it is 
"stateful" and nfs is not.  You can actually play this to your advantage 
sometimes - nfs will just keep trying to find what it is looking for 
(depending on the mount type) because it is too dumb to know that it's 
gone.  Nfs will come back when the system re-appears.  Smb knows that a 
client or server is gone and applications that are not written to 
re-start the network will have to be re-started.

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Tue Dec 13 14:18:15 2005
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Tue, 13 Dec 2005 14:18:15 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] nfs v. samba
In-Reply-To: <c834ec80512131408o49795470g1416633cd771d310@mail.gmail.com>
References: <1134509400.439f3d582c612@webmail.fiddlers-green.info>
	<c834ec80512131408o49795470g1416633cd771d310@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <f4468cae0512131418o7f8cb055w24e8bda50567bb6e@mail.gmail.com>

The 'casting' point is a valid one, though probably more involved than
just an NFS config:

##server /etc/exports:
/EXPORT/audio      10.0.0.0/255.255.255.0(ro,no_root_squash,async)

##clients /etc/fstab:
server:/EXPORT/audio       /mnt/audio    nfs   
defaults,ro,tcp,nfsvers=3,rsize=32768,wsize=32768,actimeo=30 0 0

Now all you need to do is create said directories, populate the server
with data, and mount on the client (and define your IP range..). 
CIFS/samba is more involved, and I wouldn't recommend it for simply
transferring files across Linux boxen.

/eli

I don't like the "look and feel" of Gnome and personally prefer KDE, but 
I think Linus is way off base condemning Gnome like that. *Large* parts 
of KDE have sketchy documentation, or none at all, and there is also the 
issue of the "dual licensing" of QT.

and that's not necessarily a good thing. As a matter of fact, XFCE4 is 
lighter than Gnome -- it's worth taking a look at IMHO.

Your ISP won't care. Are there really people using Linux that will need

what OS you're using, assuming it speaks Ethernet and IP.

You can use Windows 3.1, WARP, WOPR, Nintendo, Linux, whatever--as long 
as it can talk to your *gasp* Linksys */ickiness* router.

It turns out that, from the standpoint of using Pine,
the problem can be avoided by putting the SMTP server
on the appropriate line, rather than employing a
transport agent for outgoing mail. Some people believe
that Pine has security problems, but its enthusiasts
think it is secure enough. Are there any opinions
about this?

Robert "Tim" Kopp
http://analytic.tripod.com/

A fairly up-to-date reference gives the following
instructions for installing JRE on Debian:

    * Grab:
http://java.sun.com/j2se/1.5.0/download.jsp
    * apt-get install java-package fakeroot
    * fakeroot make-jpkg jre-1_5_0_02-linux-i586.bin
    * dpkg -i sun-j2re1.5_1.5.0+update02_i386.deb
    * logout and login again and check java
    * java -version

I have done #1. But, at least for stable, I can't find
"java-package." Has it been superseded by
"java-common?" Or is this writer on the wrong track?

Ah!  But you do need a source for "contrib"

  $ apt-cache show java-package
  Package: java-package
  ...
  Section: contrib/misc

Check your /etc/apt/sources.list

  deb ftp://archive.progeny.com/debian/ sarge main contrib non-free

From creswick at gmail.com  Wed Dec 14 09:13:28 2005
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Wed, 14 Dec 2005 09:13:28 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] CDParanoia/Lame issues/ Ping Paul Mullen
In-Reply-To: <439F247E.9482.C2E395AA@localhost>
References: <439F247E.9482.C2E395AA@localhost>
Message-ID: <c834ec80512140913k7fb19246r4595c618651f789e@mail.gmail.com>

On 12/13/05, John Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> But at the end, it created cdda.wav in the root. Huh? I never
> specified where it was to put cdda.wav before and it always put it in
> my home folder, overwriting the previous cdda.wav file. Maybe it put
> it in the root folder because this time I sudo'ed it. But then that
> takes me back to why cdparanoia insists it doesn't have
> permission to create cdda.wav in my home folder. Before sudo'ing
> it I gave it the command as jjj at devil5/$. Surely the owner of the
> home folder can issue a command to write a file to it.

I believe cdparanoia puts the cdda.wav (or watever output file) in the
current directory, so, if you are in your home dir (as you probably
were before) then it would deposit the file there, and do so with out
any permission issues.  Judging from the prompt you show above:

       jjj at devil5/$

You are *not* in your home directory, rather you are in the root "/". 
You probably want your prompt to look like this:

       jjj at devil5 /home/jjj$

which signifies that you are in your home directory.  (Disclaimer:
every one's prompt is different, but I'm reasonably confident I've
interpreted yours correctly).  If you want to set up a script or
something that always puts the output file in the same place, run

      cdparanoia --help

and look for the option to specify an output file there. (I would
check for you, but I don't have a copy of cdparanoia handy).

HTH,
--Rogan

> Or maybe I should have specified where cdparanoia was to create

Ubuntu offers a variation called XUbuntu that's XFCE-based.  I tried
installing XUbuntu-desktop on my regular Ubuntu 5.10 system, then
logged out of the Gnome desktop and logged into the XFCE one.  It was
OK, if less well-polished as Gnome.  What was interesting was that
once Firefox had pigged out on all the RAM, the difference in memory
consumption between the two desktops was insignificant.

All,

I upgraded my clark connect gateway and was playing around with the network
tools. I saw this connection extablished: 

TCP	01:11:34.38	192.168.2.20   69.88.120.210 	ESTABLISHED	36649->80	HTTP
TCP	01:11:30.24	192.168.2.20   69.88.120.210	ESTABLISHED	41618->80	HTTP
TCP	01:11:25.61	192.168.2.20   69.88.120.210	ESTABLISHED	33028->80	HTTP

192.168.2.20 is my desktop. I wanted to investigate further so I pointed my
webbrowser to 69.88.120.210 but got nothing. That made me curious, so I started
up nmap and ran an OS Detection, and Nmap said it was a broadband router
(although Nmap is saying that conditions weren't ideal so it's just a guess).
Now I got a little paranoid and began looking at netstat to see if port 36649,
41618, or 33028 were open on my desktop, however, nothing showed up. Since the
ports are listed as established I would figure they should be in netstat
correct?

I've also run ethereal for about 15 minutes to collect data going across my
desktop eth0, but have not found any reference to 69.88.120.210 in that
traffic. Just to be safe I shut down all outgoing ports from my desktop to
69.88.120.210. The ports however, are still listed in clarkconnect's connection
monitor output. So I guess my question is, are these just left overs that
haven't closed, or are these ports actually open? If the latter, how do I get
them closed?

thanks,
Dan Herrington

at the firewall, and the lsof output shows no open files on the local box.

On 14 Dec 2005, at 15:19, Rogan Creswick wrote:

> On 12/14/05, John Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> 
> > It takes a noobie a very long time to learn the tricks of the
> > command line. :(
> 
> You may find it easier to use something like Grip to rip the cds.  It
> will deal with the renaming and filing for you, although you may have
> considered this before.  In any case, there are almost certianly
> better options than manually ripping / encoding 100's of discs.

Actually, we;ve been through all this before. I tried Grip and gave up 
on it as uselsess for classical music. The problem is that I am not 
ripping songs. I am ripping symphonies and operas. I want all the 
tracks in one mp3 file. But sometimes a CD has more than one 
symphony on it, in which case I want just the tracks for each 
symphony in one file. I've tried ripping individual tracks and then 
making them as one "song," but it does not work well in the real 
world. After all, why would I want to listen to just the thrid 
movement of a symphony without the first, second and fourth 
movements?

So the result is that I want to rip/encode the CDs individually and 
manually. Works better for classical. Unfortunately, Grip does not 
"think" this way. It is designed for an album containing a dozen 
popular songs where the publisher included one or two cool songs 
and the rest are junk that the user wants to dump. Or at the least, 
where the user wants to arrange individual "songs" into a "playlist." 
I have yet to create a playlist, as it is largely pointless for classical.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Thu Dec 15 09:27:32 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Thu, 15 Dec 2005 09:27:32 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] postfix question:  discarding mail to nonexistent users
Message-ID: <20051215172731.GA12274@gate.kl-ic.com>


I cannot find a parameter in postfix for turning off bounces for
non-existent users.  If a spammer sends my server a mail to a
non-existent user, with a fraudulent return address, postfix 
sends a "no such user" message to the return address.  While this
is probably called for by RFCs, it is not productive behavior 
these days.  Just the mail logs are noting this is occuring are
getting up to 100MB a week. 

Is there a config parameter or other kludge for postfix that turns
off "non-existent user" bounces, and just discards these messages?

Keith

P.S.  Yes, I know there are other filtering approaches, and I
am setting those up, too.  Some (like some implementations of
greylisting) get into dependency hell, so I am going slowly.
Relying too much on any particular spam filtering method allows
for single-point failures, like old dikes in hurricanes.

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Fri Dec 16 09:01:12 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Fri, 16 Dec 2005 09:01:12 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] postfix question:  discarding mail to nonexistent users
In-Reply-To: <20051215172731.GA12274@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20051215172731.GA12274@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0512160858370.25999@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Thu, 15 Dec 2005, Keith Lofstrom wrote:

> I cannot find a parameter in postfix for turning off bounces for
> non-existent users. If a spammer sends my server a mail to a non-existent
> user, with a fraudulent return address, postfix sends a "no such user"
> message to the return address. While this is probably called for by RFCs,
> it is not productive behavior these days. Just the mail logs are noting
> this is occuring are getting up to 100MB a week.

> Is there a config parameter or other kludge for postfix that turns
> off "non-existent user" bounces, and just discards these messages?

Keith,

   Yup. I use:

         check_recipient_access hash:/etc/postfix/roleaccount_exceptions
         check_recipient_access hash:/etc/postfix/recipients
in /etc/postfix/main.cf.

   In those hash files, change the REJECT to DISCARD and they're silently
gone. I let postfix reject 'em and each day I get hundreds of attempted
messages addressed to my username with an additional 4 characters. Shrug.

Rich

Applied Ecosystem Services, Inc. (TM)   |  Impact Assessments Using Fuzzy Logic"
<http://www.appl-ecosys.com>     Voice: 503-667-4517         Fax: 503-667-8863

You didn't specify what distribution, version, and ntp version you're
running.  Try decoding what's going out on the wire during the ntpdate
runs, check to make sure you're getting a response:

# tethereal -R "udp.port == 123"

Check that ntpd is running on the remote host (success of this command
will vary by nmap version, server version)

# nmap -sU -sV IP -p 123

/eli

From znmeb at cesmail.net  Fri Dec 16 09:35:01 2005
From: znmeb at cesmail.net (M. Edward (Ed) Borasky)
Date: Fri, 16 Dec 2005 09:35:01 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] ntpdate
In-Reply-To: <1134721525.43a279f505507@webmail.fiddlers-green.info>
References: <1134721525.43a279f505507@webmail.fiddlers-green.info>
Message-ID: <43A2FAC5.5060908@cesmail.net>

What distro are you using? Gentoo more or less automatically configures 
ntp, and IIRC Debian does as well.

By the way, if you have a full NAT network, it's probably not necessary 
to have a time server on your network at all -- just synchronize 
everything to a public time server. However, if your network is isolated 
from the Internet for security reasons, you'll need to have a server.

Here's the configuration info from Gentoo -- not sure how generally 
applicable it is:

http://www.gentoo.org/doc/en/home-router-howto.xml

Time Server

Keeping your system time correct is essential in maintaining a healthy 
system. One of the most common ways of accomplishing this is with the 
Network Time Protocol (NTP) and the ntp package (which provides 
implementations for both server and client).

Many people run ntp clients on their computers. Obviously, the more 
clients in the world, the larger the load the ntp servers need to 
shoulder. In environments like home networks though, we can help keep 
the load down on public servers while still providing the proper time to 
all our computers. As an added bonus, our private updates will be a lot 
faster for the clients too! All we have to do is run a ntp server on our 
router that synchronizes itself with the public internet servers while 
providing the time to the rest of the computers in the network. To get 
started, simply emerge ntp on the router.

Code Listing 6.3: Setting up the NTP server

# nano /etc/conf.d/ntp-client
Customize if you wish but the defaults should be fine
# rc-update add ntp-client default

# nano /etc/ntp.conf
Add the follwing lines:
restrict default ignore
restrict 192.168.0.0 mask 255.255.255.0 notrust nomodify notrap
These will allow only ntp clients with an IP
address in the 192.168.0.xxx range to use your ntp server
# nano /etc/conf.d/ntpd
Customize if you wish but the defaults should be fine
# rc-update add ntpd default

# /etc/init.d/ntp-client start
# /etc/init.d/ntpd start
      

*Note: * You should make sure that you allow inbound and outbound 
communication on the ntp port (123/udp) when setting up the server. The 
client just needs outbound access on port 123 over udp.

Now, on your clients, have them emerge ntp also. However, we will just 
run the ntp client so setup is a lot simpler.

Code Listing 6.4: Setting up a NTP client

# nano /etc/conf.d/ntp-client
Change the 'pool.ntp.org' server in the NTPCLIENT_OPTS variable to '192.168.0.1'
# rc-update add ntp-client default
# /etc/init.d/ntp-client start

Date: Fri, 16 Dec 2005 09:37:13 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] postfix question:  discarding mail to nonexistent users
In-Reply-To: <20051215172731.GA12274@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20051215172731.GA12274@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <1134754633.24128.13.camel@willow.odshp.com>

On Thu, 2005-12-15 at 09:27 -0800, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> I cannot find a parameter in postfix for turning off bounces for
> non-existent users.  If a spammer sends my server a mail to a
> non-existent user, with a fraudulent return address, postfix 
> sends a "no such user" message to the return address.  While this
> is probably called for by RFCs, it is not productive behavior 
> these days.  Just the mail logs are noting this is occuring are
> getting up to 100MB a week. 

Try 'local_recipient_maps'; this will reject messages for unknown local
recipients during the SMTP session, which is even less overhead than
accepting-and-not-bouncing and without the side-effect of violating
RFCs.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
Naked Ape Consulting, Ltd

From creswick at gmail.com  Fri Dec 16 09:46:43 2005
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Fri, 16 Dec 2005 09:46:43 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] What are fstab and mtab?
In-Reply-To: <43A1A043.21784.CC971174@localhost>
References: <43A035C9.19926.C70F21D7@localhost>
	<c834ec80512141519j17751200ke310b82323eb729f@mail.gmail.com>
	<43A1A043.21784.CC971174@localhost>
Message-ID: <c834ec80512160946q29bbc912mb25daff36c77811f@mail.gmail.com>

> I don't dare disconnect the iPod as long as that warning message
> is on its screen, or its filesystem will become corrupted and I'll
> have to go find a Windows XP computer to restore it.

You should be able to disconnect it with no problems, this time.  The
danger in unplugging it while it is "in use" is that you may stop a
disk write opperation before it is completed, leaving the ipod disk in
an unstable state.  Since you aren't actually *using* the ipod (it's
just plugged in -- not mounted, and you're not actually copying data
to it) this shouldn't happen. (I say "shouldn't" because it is
*possible* that the ipod is doing something bizzare to it's filesystem
-- I don't know it's internals. )  I've unplugged mine from similar
situations with no problem.

> One additional clue: Last time I plugged in the iPod was a couple
> months ago. Since then I've added a USB pocket hard disk, which
> was plugged in when I plugged in the iPod. I'm thinking that made
> Ubuntu Breezy think the iPod was sda2 instead of its normal
> status as sda1. I unplugged the pocket disk, but that didn't change
> the error messages with iPod

That's probably the issue.  The device entries are assigned when you
plug usb devices in, so if you haven't unplugged your ipod since you
unplugged your pocket drive, it will still be on a different
/dev/sda.. device.  Try unplugging the ipod and plugging it in with
out the pocket drive plugged in.  If that works, then we can worry
about getting both devices to show up at the same time.

Someone with udev or automounter experience can help more than I cank.

--Rogan

From RParr at TemporalArts.COM  Fri Dec 16 10:13:16 2005
From: RParr at TemporalArts.COM (Randall J. Parr)
Date: Fri, 16 Dec 2005 10:13:16 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] LCD Monitor Resolution Question
In-Reply-To: <43A1A2B8.9040104@comcast.net>
References: <43A1A2B8.9040104@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <43A303BC.40108@TemporalArts.com>

Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> I'm looking for a flat screen monitor to replace my Mother-in-law's 
> big glass box monitor. As I recall, LCD monitors used to come as 
> one-resolution-is-all-I-can-do, but that something changed in the last 
> couple of years.
>
> Does anyone know just what changed (if I heard this correctly) to 
> allow LCD monitors to cleanly display any of the various resolutions 
> available for glass monitors? Or, was this just a problem with 
> laptops, and doesn't affect stand alone monitors? My Thinkpad has a 
> decent looking normal resolution of 800 x 600 and, while it will 
> display 640 x 480, it doesn't look very good - especially the fonts 
> (or maybe it's just the fonts -- I don't remember which; I just know 
> it's not too good at 640 x 480).
>
> Mom needs 640 x 480 on a 17" monitor. When I'm over there fixing 
> things for her, I usually change the resolution (this is a Win98 box) 
> while I'm working on it, but then change it back for her. So, I'm 
> hoping to find the combination of things I need to get her the 
> resolution she needs, and the flexibility to have the resolution I want.
>
> I'm willing to consider 800 x 600 on a 19" screen and see if that works.
>
First, as someone already pointed out, a 15" LCD has viewable area which 
is 15" across the large diagonal which is almost as large as a 17" CRT 
monitors viewable area of between 15 and 16 inches.

Second, I have found most people find a good LCD to be much more 
readable than even a larger CRT monitor.  For example, I find it much, 
much easier to read the 1280x1024 LCD of a Compaq Presario 16" laptop 
than to read the 1280x1024 display from any of our 19" CRTs (and they 
are very good monitors with good video cards).  Just anecdotal, but I 
believe the older folks find this to be especially true. Maybe its the 
lack of jitterness.

Third, I have also found most find it easier to read and use a higher 
resolution screen with the font size, icon size, etc. enlarged than to 
read a low resolution displayed on a large screen.  This approach 
results in  text, icons, etc. that are as big and which are much, much 
better shaped and defined.  I know, esp with older windows vintages, the 
occasional small text/icon will show up but I think you'll find the 
overall result is much preferable. 

As to LCD monitor resolutions, LCD panels all have a "native resolution" 
which it is far best to run them at.  Anything else is scaled up/down by 
the LCD electronics and display with suboptimal results at the native 
resolution.  The scaled display often bad.  I would heartily recommend 
getting your mom a 17" or even 19" LCD, running it at the native 
resolution of 1280x1024, and making everything real big for her.

R.Parr, RHCE, Temporal Arts




From luke at xifos.org  Fri Dec 16 11:14:13 2005
From: luke at xifos.org (Luke Eckley)
Date: Fri, 16 Dec 2005 11:14:13 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] ntpdate
In-Reply-To: <1134721525.43a279f505507@webmail.fiddlers-green.info>
References: <1134721525.43a279f505507@webmail.fiddlers-green.info>
Message-ID: <43A31205.5020009@xifos.org>

dan at fiddlers-green.info wrote:
> All,
> 
> I've been struggling with an ntpdate and ntpd problem for about two days. I'm
> attempting to use ntpd on my gateway as the "master" time server for my
> network. However, I can't even get ntpdate to work from the command line for
> the various ntp servers I have in my ntp.conf file. Every server seems to work
> fine in debug mode (-d option) but fails with the error " no server suitable
> for synchronization found". 

The only problem I ever have with ntp is that it will not sync if the 
time is off by 180 seconds or more. That number might be off. First 
update your time, either with date, ntpdate, or some implementations of 
ntpd.

I just installed openntpd ( a port of OpenBSD's ntpd ) on my gentoo 
machine and was able to update the time by running "ntpd -s"

Check your ntpd man page for help with a large time correction.

Luke Eckley

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Fri Dec 16 11:12:19 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Fri, 16 Dec 2005 11:12:19 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] LCD Monitor Resolution Question
In-Reply-To: <43A1A2B8.9040104@comcast.net>
References: <43A1A2B8.9040104@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20051216191219.GA27355@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Thu, Dec 15, 2005 at 09:07:04AM -0800, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> I'm looking for a flat screen monitor to replace my Mother-in-law's big 
> glass box monitor. As I recall, LCD monitors used to come as 
> one-resolution-is-all-I-can-do, but that something changed in the last 
> couple of years.
> 
> Does anyone know just what changed (if I heard this correctly) to allow 
> LCD monitors to cleanly display any of the various resolutions available 
> for glass monitors? Or, was this just a problem with laptops, and 
> doesn't affect stand alone monitors? My Thinkpad has a decent looking 
> normal resolution of 800 x 600 and, while it will display 640 x 480, it 
> doesn't look very good - especially the fonts (or maybe it's just the 
> fonts -- I don't remember which; I just know it's not too good at 640 x 
> 480).
> 
> Mom needs 640 x 480 on a 17" monitor. When I'm over there fixing things 
> for her, I usually change the resolution (this is a Win98 box) while I'm 
> working on it, but then change it back for her. So, I'm hoping to find 
> the combination of things I need to get her the resolution she needs, 
> and the flexibility to have the resolution I want.
> 
> I'm willing to consider 800 x 600 on a 19" screen and see if that works.

I've just been through the "find a flat screen monitor" quest.  I ended
up with a 1280x1024 Viewsonic VA912B from Compusa, about $300 IIRC. I
ended up using the DVI interface to get maximum display quality.

We can assume that Win98 will never get any new drivers, so all you will
be able to drive is 640 x 480 (or 800 x 600) analog from your existing
card.  I haven't seen any big flat screens with this resolution.  I also
suspect if there is any pixel aliasing the monitor will look ugly (the
VA912B does this) so you will want a display that will show a multiple
of your desired resolution, so 1280 x 1024 on a 19 inch LCD may be your
best option.  Yes, there are $500 1600 x 1200 displays out there, but I
assume you have better ways to spend your money.

Unfortunately, you are still subject to how well the monitor does the
analog capture - even if it is capturing at an exact multiple, it
might look fuzzy.  That can be a function of the video source; some
of the newest video cards do some kind of clock dithering to reduce
EMI, and that would be a pain in the butt for the monitor analog
video capture.  I guess the best suggestion I can make is to consider
a 19 inch 1280x1024 monitor, and be ready to return it if it doesn't
look good.  BTW, a good test pattern is a grid of alternating
black/white pixels in a checkerboard pattern - guaranteed to make an
LCD monitor with bad analog capture display obvious streaking and noise.

When shopping for monitors, bring along a USB thumb drive with some
pictures to look at, arranged as a web page.  Most stores are glad to
let you look at your own pictures, and you can detect lots of problems
that way.  For example, I don't care about game and video performance,
but some monitors are optimized for that, costing some static performance
and off-axis viewing angle.  Look at the monitors from the direction
of "over Mom's shoulder" - many monitors look ugly from some directions,
depending on how they arrange the magic LCD goop to optimize various
things.  Monitors differ - select the one that works best in your 
specific situation.

Sorry I can't provide more help directly answering your question ("buy
X from Y") but hopefully that helps you conduct your search.


At the risk of making a long email longer, you have another option for
Mom - set her up with a Linux box running Win98 under Win4Lin (which
is how I run the occasional windoze program).  Win4Lin does not work
with some games and 3 button mouse apps, and it is a pain in the butt
to install, but the older versions at least work on top of a native
Linux file system, and communicate through Linux, so you get all the
firewalling and Linux file security and drivers and so forth.  Right
now, I have Win4Lin running on one of my virtual desktops on my laptop,
so I can run Internet Exploder on some poorly designed websites.  The
newest versions of Win4Lin, and alternatives like VMWare, use a big
opaque file as the disk partition for Win98, which means I cannot get
directly at the files inside it from Linux.  But all this may be more
distraction than you want, even if in the long term it prevents problems
with an unsupported OS.

Keith

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Fri Dec 16 11:29:08 2005

On Fri, Dec 16, 2005 at 11:18:11AM -0800, plug_0 at robinson-west.com wrote:
> What's better, win4lin or vmware?  I'm trying the vmware 5.5
> workstation trial, but admittedly I'm chafing at the sticker price. 
> $105.00 seems awfully high, although rebooting all the time is an
> awful pain.

VMWare is probably a better product all around - I understand the
emulation is better.  I use Win4Lin version 4 because I prefer to 
store files in native Linux format.  Neither product is open source,
which can be a royal pain in the ass when connecting these to a 
kernel.

All these emulators are spendy - and VMWare used to cost more like
$400, but the price has eroded because of the competition with
Win4Lin.  If you can wait, I imagine somebody will come out with
an open source windows emulator based on Xen someday.  

Keith

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Fri Dec 16 11:37:32 2005
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)

It's worth noting that the "Free[1]" version of VMWare Player allows
running of native VMware entities.  I'm currently evaluating this to
significantly[2] reduce the licensing costs for use.  I still have
concerns of feature-set and the lifetime of this pricing model.  If
it's possible to pay only for per-session licensing for the full
version used to create and modify guest OS installations, but run the
Player unlimited-ly, then it's a significant boon to instances like
mine:

1000+ node clusters with (currently) licensing per-guest to run a
Windows app inside the Linux host.  This currently keeps us (and other
installations I've worked at) from even going this route on a
large-scale.

-1:  This is still proprietary, closed-source, and subject to
licensing restrictions.
-2:  This is IMO a good idea for VMWare, as the costs of use can sum
up to be staggering for what amounts to a 'workaround' .  The MS tax
is bad enough if you "need" it for some purpose.  If this works out
for them, they well may see more revenue from people actually buying
licenses for the product :)

/eli

I was wondering if anyone can point out why the following recipes are
failing to cause all multipart/alternative and multipart/mixed e-mails

LOGABSTRACT=all
PATH=$HOME/bin:$PATH            # munpack is in $HOME/bin.
TMPDIR=/tmp/procmail.$$
TRAP="rm -rf $TMPDIR; $TRAP"
VERBOSE=yes

:0 Wic
* ^Content-Type: multipart.*
|mkdir -p $TMPDIR

# Message is multipart/alternative.  First part is text
# and should be discarded.  Second part is HTML and should
# be converted.
:0 w
* ^Content-Type: multipart/alternative.*
|munpack -t -C $TMPDIR && \
lynx -dump -force_html $TMPDIR/part2 | \
cat -s

# Message is multipart/mixed or related.  HTML is first part.
:0 w
* ^Content-Type: multipart.*
* ! ^Content-Type: multipart/alternative.*
|munpack -t -C $TMPDIR && \
lynx -dump -force_html $DIR/part1 | \
cat -s

:0:
other

Because none of the commands output their results to a file but serve
as  filters (save mkdir which has been flagged to continue
regardless), it was my understanding that procmail would continue
processing the other rules.  It does though as you can see from this

From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Fri, 16 Dec 2005 15:40:52 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] postfix question:  discarding mail to nonexistent users
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0512160858370.25999@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <20051215172731.GA12274@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.63.0512160858370.25999@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <20051216234052.GA29447@gate.kl-ic.com>


On Thu, 15 Dec 2005, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
>I cannot find a parameter in postfix for turning off bounces for
>non-existent users. If a spammer sends my server a mail to a non-existent
>user, with a fraudulent return address, postfix sends a "no such user"
>message to the return address. While this is probably called for by RFCs,
>it is not productive behavior these days. Just the mail logs are noting
>this is occuring are getting up to 100MB a week.
> 
>Is there a config parameter or other kludge for postfix that turns
>off "non-existent user" bounces, and just discards these messages?

 
On Fri, Dec 16, 2005 at 09:01:12AM -0800, Rich Shepard wrote:
>   Yup. I use:
> 
>         check_recipient_access hash:/etc/postfix/roleaccount_exceptions
>         check_recipient_access hash:/etc/postfix/recipients
> in /etc/postfix/main.cf.
> 
>   In those hash files, change the REJECT to DISCARD and they're silently
> gone. I let postfix reject 'em and each day I get hundreds of attempted
> messages addressed to my username with an additional 4 characters. Shrug.

I'm not sure I understand.  What do the roleaccount_exceptions and
recipients files look like?  I don't have them in my postfix directory.
Do I need to provide a list of valid users?  Does postfix send anything
at all back to the fraudulent sender address?

What I would like to do is just finish the smtp handshake then
silently send everything, log file entries included, to /dev/null if
there is no user to forward the mail to.  When 99.99% of incorrectly
addressed email is the result of directory attacks looking for valid
email addresses in order to spam them, I don't want to waste network
bandwidth responding.  If the RFCs specify sending back a response,
they need to be rewritten - that is pathological behavior in 2005 AD.

When I look at access(5) for DISCARD, I see:

  DISCARD optional text...
       Claim  successful  delivery  and  silently discard the message.
       Log the optional text if specified,  otherwise  log  a  generic
       message.

I suppose if I make the optional text "u", then I get a short cryptic
log entry, and short is better than long, though I would prefer no message
at all.  But since I don't have either a roleaccount_exceptions file or a
recipients file, I don't know what DISCARD or REJECT are in relation to.

Keith

From athlonrob at axpr.net  Fri Dec 16 16:31:33 2005
From: athlonrob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Fri, 16 Dec 2005 16:31:33 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] LCD Monitor Resolution Question
In-Reply-To: <43A303BC.40108@TemporalArts.com>
References: <43A1A2B8.9040104@comcast.net> <43A303BC.40108@TemporalArts.com>
Message-ID: <43A35C65.40700@axpr.net>

Randall J. Parr wrote:
> As to LCD monitor resolutions, LCD panels all have a "native resolution"
> which it is far best to run them at.  Anything else is scaled up/down by
> the LCD electronics and display with suboptimal results at the native
> resolution.  The scaled display often bad.

I've found if you scale down in a manner that makes sense, the results
aren't so bad.  If you scale from 1024x768 (native) to 800x600, for
example, you're going to have very ugly results.  However, if you scale
down from 1600x1200 (native) to 800x600, things are fine.

I've found the LCDs at work scale down nicely either way.  They're 19"
... I don't recall the brand name, but can look it up for you on
Wednesday after I'm back from vacation if you wish.  Their native
resolution is 1280x1024 (or something similar?) but they scale down to
1024x768 without the blotchiness you see in older LCDs.  They picture
isn't as sharp, but the older eyes in the office (those over the age of
40  :-P) prefer the scaled down version to the native resolution.

Rob

# addresses that must always be accepted
postmaster@     OK
abuse@          OK

[rshepard at salmo /etc/postfix]$ less recipients
<user1>@appl-ecosys.com         OK
<user2>@appl-ecosys.com         OK
<user3>@appl-ecosys.com         OK
   ...
@appl-ecosys.com                REJECT
root@                           HOLD

   The recipients map is a list of all valid usernames for which I will accept
messages.

> What I would like to do is just finish the smtp handshake then silently
> send everything, log file entries included, to /dev/null if there is no
> user to forward the mail to. When 99.99% of incorrectly addressed email is
> the result of directory attacks looking for valid email addresses in order
> to spam them, I don't want to waste network bandwidth responding. If the
> RFCs specify sending back a response, they need to be rewritten - that is
> pathological behavior in 2005 AD.

   Well, you're certainly welcome to use DISCARD rather than REJECT. Look at
it this way: rejection generates economic activity via backbone traffic and
the electricity needed to run the 'Net. You don't want to stiffle economic
growth, do you?

> I suppose if I make the optional text "u", then I get a short cryptic log
> entry, and short is better than long, though I would prefer no message at
> all. But since I don't have either a roleaccount_exceptions file or a
> recipients file, I don't know what DISCARD or REJECT are in relation to.

   It's all relative, Keith.

   BTW, if you'd like a list of all the checks I've implemented in
/etc/postfix/main.cf I'll send it to you. The order matters, I've learned.

   FWIW, with the new tuned and tested SMTP restrictions and a tuned set of
scores and rules for SpamAssassin I'm averaging about 1 spam per day that
actually makes it to my inbox. I can easily live with this.

Rich

From johnxj at comcast.net  Fri Dec 16 17:41:43 2005
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jordan)
Date: Fri, 16 Dec 2005 17:41:43 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Permissions, ownerships and n00bie nightmares
Message-ID: <43A2FC57.1136.D1E6C021@localhost>

OK, this worked before. Now it does not. I can't make any sense 
out of what is different now and why it is not working.

What is not working? Getting new mp3s into my iPod with gtkpod. 
In the past I plugged it into a USB port, I opened gtkpod, I told 
gtkpod to read its files. Then I added new mp3s to the list in gtkpod 
and told it to Sync. It synced. Done.

Now it does not automatically mount. But bear with me here, 
because my understanding of when something is mounted and 
when it is not is dim. E.g., if I can drag a file to and from a drive, I 
figure it is mounted. But I have been told that is not necessarily 
true. I don't understand why. I do understand the filesystem and 
mount points v. Windows way of doing things. I just don't 
understand when something is mounted.

So anyway, I plug in the iPod. I do "mount" and note that it is not 
mounted. So then I do "mount /media/iPod." This gives me an error 
message that special device sda2 does not exist. Like hell it 
doesn't. I even held the iPod up in front of the monitor so Ubuntu 
could get a good look at it. Doesn't exist. Bah. Stupid computer.

So finally I figure out that the command line seems to want the 
whole nine yards. It wants "mount /dev/sda -t vfat /media/iPod." But 
even that generated an error message. You see, just because I'm 
the one who paid for the computer doesn't mean I get to use it. At 
least, not as far as Linux is concerned. No. I have to mount the 
iPod as root. Otherwise I am just a worthless no-count user.

OK, so I do "sudo mount /dev/sda2 -t vfat /media/iPod." Yay! It's 
mounted! (Or seems to be.)

OK then I open gtkpod, it reads the iPod's database, and I proceed 
to add some new mp3s. Then I do Sync. And for each new mp3 I 
get:

"Error opening 
'/media/iPod/iPod_Control/Music/F12/gtkpod561325.mp3' for 
writing (Permission denied)."

What the hell? OK, I go to Konqueror and check permissions. Sure 
enough, the iPod is owned by root. Well, of course it is. Ubuntu 
required that I be root in order to mount it. But remember, gtkpod is 
being run by a mere worthless no-count user. So gtkpod can't write 
to the iPod. Why did this work before?

So what should I do? Create a new root account and just run 
everything as root from now on forever? Yeah, yeah, I know, you're 
not ever supposed to do that. OK, I won't. But the current situation 
is driving me insane. Plus, I really, really need my iPod. Because 
of the annoying Christmas music this time of year, I can't listen to 
the radio -- for the health of the radio, you understand -- because if I 
have to listen to one more little tin soldier I'm going to take a 
shotgun to the radio. And there are nine more days until this 
pseudo-religious madness is over. 

OK, getting back to resolving the problem. The last time I mounted 
my iPod and added stuff with gtkpod was a couple months ago. 
Back then I had 64-bit Hoary. Now I have 64-bit Breezy. So maybe 
Breezy broke something. But what? Suspicion: It broke the 
automount feature. But all I know about automount is that it exists 
and it's some cool feature of recent releases. 

So first, I need someone to tell me if my thinking is anywhere close 
to correct, that is, that the problem may lie with automount. And 
second, how to fix automount. And third, any other suggestions, 
including the fact that (as usual) I'm a hopeless n00bie who keeps 
screwing up his computer.

From chaz at bctonline.com  Fri Dec 16 18:04:58 2005

Having the mount point owned by root is not a problem although it should
allow read and search permissions for everyone (rwx r-x r-x, or 755).
Your mount directive did not mount the device read-only so that should be ok
too.
Is it possible to look at the dirs/files on the device to see what their
permissions are using tools like 'ls' ?

From cstevens at gencom.us  Fri Dec 16 18:10:21 2005
From: cstevens at gencom.us (D. Cooper Stevenson)
Date: Fri, 16 Dec 2005 18:10:21 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Permissions, ownerships and n00bie nightmares
In-Reply-To: <43A2FC57.1136.D1E6C021@localhost>
References: <43A2FC57.1136.D1E6C021@localhost>
Message-ID: <1134785421.29403.5.camel@localhost>

John,

I feel your pain. The good news is that you've hit upon what is perhaps
the toughest permissions related hurdle for new users: default mounting
permissions.

There's a reason why mount works the way it does, that's another post.
Try one of the following (be certain to unmount the iPod volume first):

  sudo mount -t vfat -o umask=000 /dev/sda2 /media/iPod

Or (better yet):

sudo mount -t vfat -o uname=[your_user_name] /dev/sda2 /media/iPod

Send an 'ls -ld' of your /media/iPod directory if you still are having
trouble. 


-Coop

From johnxj at comcast.net  Fri Dec 16 18:47:24 2005
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jordan)

On 16 Dec 2005, at 18:04, Charles Sliger wrote:

> Having the mount point owned by root is not a problem although it
> should allow read and search permissions for everyone (rwx r-x r-x, or
> 755). Your mount directive did not mount the device read-only so that
> should be ok too. Is it possible to look at the dirs/files on the
> device to see what their permissions are using tools like 'ls' ?

Thanks for the pointer. That makes sense.

Yes, I can navigate to the directories on the iPod (as jjj) using the 
command line. I can see anything that is there. However, the ls 
command does not list the permissions, just the folder/file names. I 
did ls --help to see if there was an option to display permissions, 
but didn't see one.

However, Konqueror does list the permissions. There are four 
folders on the iPod (Calendars, Contacts, iPod_Control, and 
Notes). They are all rwxr-xr-x. The Calendars, Contacts and Notes 
folders are unused. The iPod_Contol folder has three folders 
(Device, iTunes and Music). They are also all rwxr-xr-x. And each 
has files and/or subfolders, and all are also rwxr-xr-x. Evidently the 
whole thing is rwxr-xr-x.

However, I don't know the syntax for what rwxr-xr-x means. Is this 
a bad thing? If so, is it possible to change it?

On 16 Dec 2005, at 18:10, D. Cooper Stevenson wrote:

> I feel your pain. The good news is that you've hit upon what is
> perhaps the toughest permissions related hurdle for new users: default
> mounting permissions.

<Sigh>

> There's a reason why mount works the way it does, that's another post.
> Try one of the following (be certain to unmount the iPod volume
> first):

Now I can't unmount it.

umount /dev/iPod gives me "umount: /media/iPod disagrees with 
the fstab." That damn fstab again. 

So I tried "umount /dev/sda2" and got "umount: /dev/sda2 is not 
mounted (according to mtab)." Ah, god old mtab. If fstab doesn't 
screw me up, mtab will step in and make sure I remain powerless 
over this computer.

But if I run "mount," the last line says it is mounted. Oh wait, it 
says sdb2, not sda2. OK, got it unmounted finally with "sudo 
umount /dev/sdb2."

>   sudo mount -t vfat -o umask=000 /dev/sda2 /media/iPod

Omigod, it WORKED! As I sit here gtkpod is finally syncing!

OK, I had to change your line to sdb2. So now that the immediate 
problem is resolved, it's time to see if I can learn something from 
this.

1) Why is it sometimes sda2 and sometimes sdb2?  

2) I know what the -t vfat does (although the documentation says it 
should not be necessary). And I understand the device is /dev/sdb2 
and the mount point is /media/iPod. But what does "-o 
umask=000" do?

3) Any theories as to why it automounted and worked without any 
problems a couple months ago, and now requires a PhD in 
CompSci to get it working?

> Or (better yet):
> 
> sudo mount -t vfat -o uname=[your_user_name] /dev/sda2 /media/iPod

I have saved all the correspondence, for future reference. You 
swee, I know that next time I try to mount the iPod something else 
will be screwed up.

Thanks!

I recently had to go through this with my mother-in-law.  I got her a
new computer for Christmas and I did not want to have to deal with
Windows and to ensure that she updated on a regular basis so I
installed SuSE 10.  At first, she was not sure about the change, but
it only took 1 week of playing around with it, before she felt
comfortable.  She found OO was so similar to word that there was no
problem using OO.  The big bonus is that I setup the system so that I
can SSH to the system remotely if she has any problem, I do not have
to travel to her house to resolve any issues.  I did have to install
JRE-5 on the system so that she could play her YAHOO games (which I
did remotely :)).

From ehem at m5p.com  Fri Dec 16 19:42:31 2005
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Fri, 16 Dec 2005 19:42:31 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Kernel configuration: Sarge
In-Reply-To: <20051216165727.AC2974805@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200512170342.jBH3gVdp008941@m5p.com>

>From: Robert Kopp <iconoklastic at yahoo.com>
> You're not supposed to trifle with the kernel in
> Debian, I guess. After installing, say, Fedora, typing
> "xconfig" would pop the window right up, but that's
> not the case here (Sarge). The kernel source was
> missing, and that's easy to correct. But then "make
> xconfig"

No distribution particularly likes you playing with the kernel. Just
differing distributions install differing percentages of the packages
needed for kernel building by default. Since Fedora isn't aimed at end
users it make sense for them to install development packages by default,
while Debian which aims to be a stable base doesn't.

The "proper" way to do kernels on Debian is to install the
"kernel-package" package. You then use the script "make-kpkg" as a
front-end to the kernel build, and it produces a Debian package for you.

> *
> * Unable to find the QT installation. Please make sure
> that the
> * QT development package is correctly installed and
> the QTDIR
> * environment variable is set to the correct location.
> *
> make[1]: *** [scripts/kconfig/.tmp_qtcheck] Error 1
> make: *** [xconfig] Error 2
> 
> What's the QT installation? And what's the QT
> development package? And what's the correct location
> for the QTDIR environment variable? The query 
> 
> apt-cache search qt 

One wonders if perhaps this was a precursor to Linus going for KDE.
Perhaps they bribed Linus by writing the qconfig script, while the GTK
folks got into the game late.

libqt3-mt-dev

> must have located at least 50 packages. Well, "config"
> is dreadful, so I wanted to make sure that at least
> "menuconfig" would work. But that complained that
> ncurses-dev was absent. There is no such package, but
> libncurses5-dev must be what they mean, for that
> works.

Installing "kernel-package" would of sucked that in as a suggested
package (though you could of told the system to disregard that).

> But suppose I'm a perfectionist? What still needs to
> be done to get into qconf? I once used it with Fedora,
> and it's neat.

Installing "kernel-package", using `make-kpkg`, and installing the
package produced is the right way. You've also got the option to install
"libgtk2.0-dev" and use gconfig which is the GTK front-end.

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Sat Dec 17 14:19:01 2005
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Sat, 17 Dec 2005 14:19:01 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] What are fstab and mtab?
Message-ID: <200512171419.01711.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from John Jordan
# on Thursday 15 December 2005 04:56 pm:

<lots of snipping in the following quoted text>

>Previously it auto-mounted. All I had to be careful of
>was to issue the command "umount /media/iPod" before
>disconnecting it.
>
> /dev/sda2 does not exist."
>
>Does the last error message mean it is not listed in fstab or mtab?
>If so, how does it get in there
>
>Since then I've added a USB pocket hard disk,
>
>I don't dare disconnect the iPod
>
>This sucks.

Do you hate automounters yet?  I know I do.  IMO, a great way to find 
trouble is to plug and unplug disks without knowing what the OS is up 
to (the same goes for windows -- which sucks even more because you have 
no way to disable this silly opaque behavior.)

Read the manpages for mount, umount, and fstab.

If you don't like reading manpages on the console (I know I don't) just 
put "man:fstab" in the address bar of konqueror.

Ok, just read the first line of "man fstab".

Note:  it says "fstab - static information about the filesystems"

Yeah, that sucks, but that's the way it is, and it is pretty stable and 
transparent even if it doesn't mesh well with usb disks.

If you disable your automounter, you only have to put a line 
in /etc/fstab for every disk that you want to mount.  The trouble with 
this when it comes to usb disks is that they are /dev/sd<whatever 
letter comes next> depending on the order in which you have plugged 
them in, when you last rebooted, day of the week, price of stamps, lunar 
phase, etc.

That's no fun.

What *is* fun is udev.

Maybe it's too complicated to just hold your nose and dive in without 
completely wrecking your system and having to use vi from the command 
line to recover (but isn't that everything that's cool about Linux?)

Anyway, if you're not afraid of that (or at least you know someone who 
has the skills to resurrect your box from a bricklike state), you just 
have to figure out the serial number/model or some other identifying 
information for each usb disk.  This is most easily done by 
watching /var/log/messages as you plug it in and then doing something 
like:

  cat /sys/bus/usb/devices/4-4/product

(Where I really do mean _like_ because the magic number 4-4 comes from 
the very messy /devices/pci0000:00/blah-blah/<magic-number>/blah-blah 
string that "tail /var/log/messages" tells you.)

product contains something like:

  "USB DISK 2.0"

There's also "serial", "idVendor" and some other interesting stuff in 
there.  Look around, it's just some text files, symlinks, and 
directories.  Hmm... transparent... drool...

Ok, so now you put something _like_ this in /etc/udev/udev.rules.

BUS="usb", KERNEL="sd*", SYSFS{product}="USB DISK 2.0", NAME="%k", 
SYMLINK="mydisk%n"

And, finally, the next time you plug in that usb disk, udev will 
create /dev/mydisk, /dev/mydisk1,  and so on (though you probably only 
have one partition.)

Now you can put a _static_ line in that file that contains "static 
information about the filesystems" and just do mount /media/mydisk, 
umount /media/mydisk and _know_ what is happening with your disks 
rather than wondering if they are currently being eaten by your 
automounter.

You can do lots of other fun stuff with udev too, including 
automatically mounting the disk, starting kteatime (though you might 
need dcop for that?) whenever you plug in the usb teawarmer, etc.

--Eric
-- 
You see, Perl's really a big language and it's not a toy.
--Ovid

    http://scratchcomputing.com

On Sat, Dec 17, 2005 at 08:16:27PM -0800, Russell Senior wrote:
> How much voltage should there be on a dead battery?  I just measured
> 13.15 and 13.2 V, respectively.  PowerSonic PS-12120 F2.

Looks OK, though often a bad battery can show a good voltage, but fail
a load test.  Short the battery for a half-second with a long piece of
wire and then measure the voltage.  Or see if you can monitor the
battery voltage during a UPS test.  Or you could put the UPS into
Runtime Calibration mode.

The UPS probably uses standard-issue rectangular 12V 7AH gel-cells,
which you can buy at any business with "Batteries" in the name or at
Fry's; you can also take a chance on some used ones from Wacky
Willy's.  All are open Sunday.

Heck, if it's for your business, buy a new one and expense it this
year.  (Section 179 depreciation, or as my Econ prof calls it,
"daddy's little helper".) CompUSA has an APC BX1500 on sale for $150.

On Sat, 17 Dec 2005, Mike Witt wrote:

> Ok, well since it sounds like a couple of people are using it... One 
> thing I'm stuck is is that I can't get notifications working for 
> services. (They work for hosts.)

What version of nagios are you running? I'm using 2.0b3; if you're 
somewhere in that ballpark, then these snippets may be helpful:

define service {
   name                  enterprise-service
   register              0
   check_period          24x7
   contact_groups        admins
   max_check_attempts    3
   normal_check_interval 30
   notification_interval 60
   notification_options  w,c,r
   notification_period   24x7
   retry_check_interval  2
   }

   [....]

define service {
   host_name             BlahBlah
   service_description   SMTP server
   check_command         check_smtp
   use                   enterprise-service
   }

I'd also be interested to know if anything shows up in your system 
logs. Does nagios try to send messages but get thwarted by local SMTP 
configs? Do you have a smarthost somewhere in your e-mail chain that's 
refusing to relay?

From sbeattie at suse.de  Sun Dec 18 03:06:54 2005
From: sbeattie at suse.de (Steve Beattie)
Date: Sun, 18 Dec 2005 03:06:54 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Need help w/ a procmail recipe
In-Reply-To: <e3b7bac0512161235v34a5444ds5561677acf26e9db@mail.gmail.com>
References: <e3b7bac0512161235v34a5444ds5561677acf26e9db@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20051218110654.GA1160@immunix.com>

On Fri, Dec 16, 2005 at 12:35:10PM -0800, Travis Spencer wrote:
> I was wondering if anyone can point out why the following recipes are
> failing to cause all multipart/alternative and multipart/mixed e-mails
> to be converted to plain text and output to the mailbox called other:
> 
> LOGABSTRACT=all
> PATH=$HOME/bin:$PATH            # munpack is in $HOME/bin.
> TMPDIR=/tmp/procmail.$$
> TRAP="rm -rf $TMPDIR; $TRAP"
> VERBOSE=yes
> 
> :0 Wic
> * ^Content-Type: multipart.*
> |mkdir -p $TMPDIR
> 
> # Message is multipart/alternative.  First part is text
> # and should be discarded.  Second part is HTML and should
> # be converted.
> :0 w
> * ^Content-Type: multipart/alternative.*
> |munpack -t -C $TMPDIR && \
> lynx -dump -force_html $TMPDIR/part2 | \
> cat -s
> 
> # Message is multipart/mixed or related.  HTML is first part.
> :0 w
> * ^Content-Type: multipart.*
> * ! ^Content-Type: multipart/alternative.*
> |munpack -t -C $TMPDIR && \
> lynx -dump -force_html $DIR/part1 | \
> cat -s
> 
> :0:
> other
> 
> Because none of the commands output their results to a file but serve
> as  filters (save mkdir which has been flagged to continue
> regardless), it was my understanding that procmail would continue
> processing the other rules.  It does though as you can see from this
> log entry:

It can, but you have to tell procmail to treat the pipe as a filter;
otherwise it expects the piped command to do the final delivery. For a
piped delivery example, my spamassassin capture rule looks like so:

  # SpamAssasin adds a header for stuff it thinks is spam.
  :0:
  * ^X-Spam-Status: Yes,
  | gzip >> spam.archive/spam-${MONTH}.gz

because I like to keep an archive of it around, but prefer it take up
as little space as possible.

Perusing my own .procmailrc, all the rules I have that act as filters
are prefixed with:

  :0 fw

rather than just the bare:

  :0 w

that you have, so you might try that.

A couple of other misc notes, not related to the issue you're having:

  - you might wrap up everything into a script, so that you don't have
    to have that funny rule+trap to mkdir/rmdir.
  - /tmp/procmail.$$ is a pretty guessable/DOSable path, use mktemp(1)
    (and create/use a ~/tmp/ as well)

HTH.
-- 
Steve Beattie
SUSE Labs, Novell Inc. 
<sbeattie at suse.de>
http://NxNW.org/~steve/

From pdxlinux1 at langenberg.com  Sun Dec 18 16:08:29 2005
From: pdxlinux1 at langenberg.com (Chuck Langenberg)
Date: Sun, 18 Dec 2005 16:08:29 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Startup Question (warning this question mentions Windoze)
References: <43A5D48B.5020905@frankhunt.com>
Message-ID: <000f01c60430$b676ad10$c2938843@chuck4>

Frank:

. The main thing I do is to swap components in-and-out one-at-a-time, to
  help identify when a problem began.
. Get the system down to as bare bones as possible, while still being able
  to boot. Disconnect all of the components you can; including NIC card,
  sound card, pull out all of the DIMMs but one, and "ESPECIALLY"
  disconnect the CD ROM cable from the motherboard (I wish I could count
  the number of times that the mere existence of a particular CD drive was
  the source of a problem, and these issues were all so dissimilar).
. If the problem persists, try rotating the DIMMs around, one-at-a-time.
. If it "DOES" boot, then the issue was "probably" caused by one of the
  components you just removed.
. Once you've got a system that boots, try re-introducing the components
  "one-at-a-time" until it "doesn't" boot again -- at which point you've
  identified the likely component that's causing you grief.
. I always re-insert cards into different slots than before.
. If your booting issue persists with everything removed, and while
  everything is still removed, try resetting the BIOS to the defaults.
. I will be interested to hear your resolution.

Warm regards,
Chuck Langenberg
